The Ruling: Denying the Prophet's Universal Mission is Disbelief
The author gives the ruling on denying the Prophet's mission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that denying any part of the Prophet’s mission that is known necessarily from religion renders one a disbeliever according to Ahl al-Sunna.

Core Idea
Rejecting the universal message is like rejecting Islam itself.

This lesson presents the doctrinal consequence of denying the universality of the Prophet’s mission. The author rules that whoever denies his mission in whole or in part (where that part is known by necessity) is a disbeliever. The condition is that the person is legally responsible and the message has reached them.

Guided Path
1
Read the ruling
Read the Arabic statement carefully. Note the conditional structure: 'whoever denies... is a disbeliever... if legally responsible and the call reached him.'
2
Understand 'entirely or partially'
The footnote clarifies that 'partially' refers to matters known necessarily from the religion, such as the universality of the message.
3
Appreciate the implication
Reflect on why denying a necessary part of the religion is equated with outright denial of Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إرساله إلى الملائكة الحيوانات والجمادات(۱) ، حتَّى إلى نفسه (۲)، وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَاكَ إِلَّا كَافَةً لِلنَّاسِ (سبأ: ۲۸) وفيه ردُّ على العيسوية (۳) من اليهود حيثُ (۱) قوله: (وجميع الحيوانات والجمادات والإرسال إلى الحيوانات نجاة وأمان من الخسف والمَسخ ، فقد كان يُخْسِفُ بها في الأمم الماضية ، وقوله : ( والجمادات) أي: فآمنت به ، ولا مانع من أن الله يودعها الإدراك ، وفائدة ذلك أنها صارت مأمونة مِن أنْ تُساقَ إلى جهنم ويعذب بها أهلها، فحجارة جهنم هي غير الحجارة التي على ظهر الدنيا، فإن قلت: من جملة الجمادات الأصنام، وقد ورد أنها تُدخل مع عُبَادِها ، فالجوب: أنها وإن دخلتها لكن لا يعذب بها ، فدخولها للتنكيل عليهم الشنواني . (۲) قوله : (حتى إلى نفسه بأن يُذعن وجوبا أنه رسول الله صدقه بالمعجزات. الشنواني. (۳) العيسوية من يهود أصبهان، نسبوا إلى أبي عيسى إسحاق بن يعقوب الأصفهاني. وقيل: إن اسمه: عوفيد ألوهيم أي عابد الله . كان في زمن المنصور، وابتدأ دعوته في زمن آخر ملوك بني أمية مروان بن محمد الحمار ، فاتبعه بشرٌ كثير من اليهود، وادعوا له آيات ومعجزات ، زعم أبو عيسى أنه نبي ؛ وأنه رسول المسيح المنتظر . وزعم أن للمسيح خمسة من الرسل يأتون قبله واحدا بعد واحد وهو آخرهم. وزعم أن الله تعالى كلمه، وكلَّفَهُ أَنْ يُخلص بني إسرائيل من أيدي الأمم العاصين والملوك الظالمين. وزعم أن المسيح أفضل ولد آدم، وأنه أعلى منزلة من الأنبياء الماضين، وإذ هو رسوله فهو أفضل الكل أيضا، وخالف اليهود في كثير من أحكام الشريعة الكثيرة المذكورة في التوراة. وقد حارب المسلمين وقتل كثيرا منهم ، والعيسوية يقرُّونَ بنبوة نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وبأن كل ما جاء به حق ؛ ولكنهم زعموا أنه بعث إلى العرب خاصة لا إلى بني إسرائيل. انظر: «الفرق بين الفرق» و«الملل والنحل . فائدة: ردَّ الإمام حجة الإسلام الغزالي على زعم العيسوية فقال في كتابه «الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد حيث ذهبوا إلى أنه الرسول إلى العرب فقط لا إلى غيرهم، وهذا ظاهر البطلان ؛ فإنهم اعترفوا بكونه رسولا حقا، ومعلوم أن الرسول لا يكذب، وقد ادعى هو أنه رسول مبعوث إلى الثقلين ، وبَعثَ رُسُله إلى كسرى وقيصر وسائر ملوك العجم، وتواتر ذلك منه ، فما قالوه محال متناقض). قسم النبوات زعموا تخصيص رسالته بالعرب ، ومَن نفى بعثته كُلًّا أو بعضا (۱) كَمَن نفى الإسلام كذلك فهو كافر عند الأشاعرة (۲) إن كان مُكلَّفا وبلغته الدعوة (۱) قوله: (كلا) أي: إن أنكر بعثته من أصلها وقوله : (أو بعضا) أي: بشرط أن يكون معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة. الشنواني . (٢) أي عند أهل السنة مطلقا . الشنواني .
His sending to the angels, animals, and inanimate objects (1), even to himself (2), and His statement Exalted is He: “And We have not sent you except comprehensively to mankind” [Saba: 28]; and in it is a refutation of the ‘Isawiyya (3) from the Jews, who (1) His statement: “and all the animals and inanimate objects – and sending to the animals is deliverance and safety from being swallowed and metamorphosed, for that used to happen to them in past nations, and His statement: ‘and inanimate objects’ means: so they believed in him, and there is no impediment to Allah endowing them with perception, and the benefit of that is that they become safe from being driven to Hell and its inhabitants being punished by them, for the stones of Hell are other than the stones on the surface of the earth; if you say: among the inanimate objects are idols, and it has come that they are entered with their worshippers, the answer is: that even if they enter it, they are not punished by them, for their entry is for humiliation upon them – al-Shanwani. (2) His statement: “even to himself” meaning that he must necessarily acknowledge that he is the Messenger of Allah, verified by miracles – al-Shanwani. (3) The ‘Isawiyya are from the Jews of Isfahan, traced to Abu ‘Isa Ishaq ibn Ya‘qub al-Isfahani. It is said his name was ‘Ufayd Aluhim, i.e., servant of Allah. He was in the time of al-Mansur, and began his call in the time of the last Umayyad kings Marwan ibn Muhammad al-Himar, and many Jews followed him, and they claimed for him signs and miracles. Abu ‘Isa claimed that he was a prophet, and that he was the messenger of the awaited Messiah. He claimed that the Messiah has five messengers who come before him one after another, and he is the last of them. He claimed that Allah spoke to him and charged him to deliver the Children of Israel from the hands of the disobedient nations and oppressive kings. He claimed that the Messiah is the best of the children of Adam, and that he is of higher station than the past prophets, and since he is his messenger, he is also the best of all. He opposed the Jews in many of the many rulings of the law mentioned in the Torah. He fought the Muslims and killed many of them. The ‘Isawiyya affirm the prophethood of our Prophet Muhammad (peace and blessings be upon him) and that everything he brought is true, but they claimed that he was sent specifically to the Arabs, not to the Children of Israel. See: “al-Farq bayn al-Firaq” and “al-Milal wa al-Nihal”. Benefit: The Imam, Proof of Islam, al-Ghazali, refuted the claim of the ‘Isawiyya and said in his book “al-Iqtisad fi al-I‘tiqad” where they held that he is the messenger only to the Arabs, not to others, and this is manifest falsehood; for they acknowledged that he is truly a messenger, and it is known that a messenger does not lie, and he claimed that he is a messenger sent to the two weighty ones (humans and jinn), and he sent his messengers to Kisra and Caesar and the rest of the kings of the non-Arabs, and that is mutawatir from him, so what they said is impossible and contradictory. Section on Prophethoods. They claimed the specification of his message to the Arabs, and whoever denies his mission entirely or partially (1) is like whoever denies Islam likewise, so he is a disbeliever according to the Ash‘aris (2) if he is legally responsible and the call reached him. (1) His statement: “entirely” i.e., if he denies his mission from its origin, and his statement “or partially” i.e., on condition that it is known necessarily from the religion – al-Shanwani. (2) i.e., according to Ahl al-Sunna absolutely – al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation and ruling. First, it asserts the universal scope of the Prophet’s mission: it includes angels, animals, inanimate objects, and even himself (meaning his own soul must acknowledge his prophethood). The verse from Surah Saba is cited as evidence: “We have not sent you except comprehensively to mankind.” The author then identifies the opposing group, the ‘Isawiyya (a Jewish sect from Isfahan), providing a detailed account of their founder Abu ‘Isa and their beliefs. The key point is that they accept the Prophet’s prophethood but restrict it to Arabs, claiming he was not sent to the Children of Israel. This is refuted by Imam al-Ghazali, who argues that since they admit he is a true messenger, and he himself claimed universality (sent to both humans and jinn) and acted on it by sending letters to royalty, their position is self-contradictory. The passage then moves to a doctrinal ruling: whoever denies the Prophet’s mission in whole or in part (where the part is known necessarily from religion) is a disbeliever according to the Ash‘aris (i.e., Ahl al-Sunna). The footnotes clarify that “partially” means what is known by necessity, and the ruling applies if the person is legally responsible and the call has reached them.

Key Terms
كافر
أشاعرة
معلوم من الدين بالضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think only outright denial of prophethood is disbelief, not partial denial.
Correction: The passage states that denying even a part known by necessity (like universality) is disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on someone who denies that the Prophet was sent to the jinn (a matter known by necessity)?
A) He is a disbeliever if the call reached him and he is legally responsible
B) He is sinful but remains a Muslim
C) He is excused because it is not explicitly mentioned
D) He is considered an innovator

Key Takeaway
Denying any necessary part of the Prophet's mission is disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you fully accept every aspect of the Prophet's mission that is known by necessity from the religion, or do you find yourself selectively rejecting or downplaying parts that conflict with your desires or social circle? What would it cost you to surrender completely?
?
If you encountered a Muslim who denies the universal mission of the Prophet (e.g., claiming the message was only for Arabs), how would you engage with them? Would you fear their reaction more than you fear Allah's judgment?
?
Are there any 'partial' teachings of Islam that you treat as optional or non-binding because they are inconvenient? What does your willingness to reject them say about the reality of your iman?

Accidental vs Original Universality in Prophetic Missions
The author compares Noah's accidental universality with the Prophet's original universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the accidental universality of Noah's mission after the flood and the original universal mission of Prophet Muhammad, and how this refutes the objection that Noah's mission was equally universal.

Core Idea
Noah's universal mission was accidental; the Prophet's was original and includes jinn.

Some might argue that since Noah was sent to all humans after the flood, his mission was as universal as the Prophet's. In this passage, the author clarifies that Noah's universality was circumstantial, not part of his original commission. Moreover, Noah was not sent to the jinn, while the Prophet was. Understanding this nuance is crucial for appreciating the unique status of the final Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: عموم, اتفاقي, تسخير.
The passage includes a contrast between Noah and Solomon.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that Noah's universality is described as 'ittifāqī' (accidental/agreed-upon) because it resulted from the post-flood situation. In contrast, the Prophet's universality is original, from the start of his mission.
The author also mentions that Noah was not sent to jinn, unlike the Prophet.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this distinction answers the objection that Noah's mission was equally universal. Also note that Solomon's power over jinn was kingly, not prophetic, which further highlights the uniqueness of the Prophet's mission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عموم رسالة نوح على نبينا وعليه الصلاة والسَّلام بعد الطوفان (۳) فأمر اتفاقي (٤) ؛ لأنَّه لم يَسْلُمْ مِن الهلاك إلَّا مَن كان معه في السَّفينة، على أنه لم يرسل للجن (٥) ، وأمَّا تسخير الجن والإنس لسليمان على نبينا وعليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام فهو تسخيرُ سَلطَنَة وملك لا تسخير نبوّة. (۳) قوله : ( بعد الطوفان ظاهره أنها قبل الطوفان لم تكن عامة، وقيل: بل عامة ؛ وإلا لما صح إغراق الجميع. الأمير. (٤) هذا جواب عما قد يقال : إن تعميم البعثة ليس خاصا بنبينا بل مثله نوح، فإنه كان مبعوثا لجميع من في الأرض بعد الطوفان ، وحاصل الجواب : أن هذا العموم لم يكن في أصل بعثة ، وإنما وقع لأجل ما حدث من انحصار الخلق في الموجودين بهلاك سائر الناس، وأما نبينا له و عموم رسالته في أصل البعثة ، وذلك خاص به، وقد يقال: إن التعميم خاص بزمن نوح فقط ، فلا يساوي التعميم الذي لنبينا، فإن تعميم رسالته لكل زمان بعده ، فأين التعميم الخاص من التعميم العام ؟ على أنَّ سيدنا نوحاً لم يُرسل إلى الجن، فإنه لم يرسل لهم إلا نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وأما تسخير الجن لسليمان عليه السلم فتسخير سلطنة وملك لا تسخير نبوة الشنواني والباجوري في التحفة . (٥) قوله : ( على أنه لم يرسل للجن) هو الأحسنُ في الجواب، لاقتضاء الأول أنَّ المغرقين لم يُرسل لهم سيدنا نوح ، فيقال : إذا كان لم يرسل لهم فما موجب غرقهم ؟ . بعض الشنواني .
As for the generality of the mission of Noah (peace and blessings be upon him and our Prophet) after the flood (3), it is an agreed-upon matter (4); because no one survived the destruction except those who were with him in the ship, although he was not sent to the jinn (5). And as for the subjugation of the jinn and humans to Solomon (peace and blessings be upon him and our Prophet), it is a subjugation of sultanate and kingship, not a subjugation of prophethood. (3) His statement: “after the flood” – its apparent meaning is that before the flood it was not general, and it is said: rather it was general; otherwise the drowning of all would not be valid – al-Amir. (4) This is an answer to what might be said: The generalization of the mission is not specific to our Prophet, but similarly Noah, for he was sent to all those on earth after the flood. The gist of the answer: that this generality was not in the origin of his mission, but rather occurred due to the confinement of creation to those existing after the destruction of the rest of humanity. And as for our Prophet (peace be upon him), the generality of his message is in the origin of his mission, and that is specific to him. It may also be said: The generalization was specific to the time of Noah only, so it does not equal the generalization that is for our Prophet, for the generalization of his message is for every time after him. So where is the specific generalization compared to the absolute generalization? Moreover, our master Noah was not sent to the jinn, for none was sent to them except our Prophet Muhammad (peace and blessings be upon him). And as for the subjugation of the jinn to Solomon (peace be upon him), it is a subjugation of sultanate and kingship, not a subjugation of prophethood – al-Shanwani and al-Bajuri in al-Tuhfa. (5) His statement: “although he was not sent to the jinn” is the best in the answer, because the first [point] implies that the drowned were not sent to by our master Noah, so it is said: if he was not sent to them, then what is the cause of their drowning? – some [commentary?] – al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a comparative discussion on the universality of prophetic missions. It addresses a potential objection: if the Prophet’s mission is universal, was not Noah’s also universal after the flood? The author responds that Noah’s universality was accidental (ittifāqī), not original, because after the flood only those in the ark survived, so his message effectively reached all remaining humans. In contrast, the Prophet’s universality is from the beginning of his mission. Additionally, Noah was not sent to the jinn, while the Prophet was. The passage also distinguishes between Solomon’s control over jinn and humans, which was a kingly power, not a prophetic sending. The footnotes clarify that Noah’s pre-flood mission was not universal, and that saying he was not sent to the jinn is the best answer to the objection about why the drowned were punished if they were not sent to.

تسخير سلطنة
تسخير نبوة

إنس
سليمان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that Noah's mission was as universal as the Prophet's because he was sent to all humans after the flood.
Correction: Noah's universality was accidental, resulting from the post-flood situation, and he was not sent to the jinn. The Prophet's mission is originally universal and includes both humans and jinn.

Checkpoint
Why is Noah's post-flood universality considered 'agreed upon' (ittifāqī) rather than original?
A) Because he was originally sent only to his people, but after the flood all surviving humans were his addressees.
B) Because all scholars agree that his mission was universal.
C) Because he was sent to both humans and jinn.
D) Because his message was only for the period after the flood, not before.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet Muhammad's mission is unique in being originally universal and including jinn, unlike Noah's accidental post-flood universality and Solomon's kingly authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the Prophet's message is for every human and jinn until the end of time? If so, how does that influence your daily interactions and your sense of responsibility to convey Islam?
?
When you think of the universality of Islam, does it fill you with a sense of urgency to share it with others, or do you treat it as a theoretical belief? What specific step can you take this week to act on this belief?
?
Consider Noah's accidental universality: his message reached all because of a physical flood. The Prophet's message reaches all by divine design. How does this distinction affect your confidence in Islam's truth and your role in its spread? What are you personally doing to ensure that the message reaches those around you who may not know it?

Ruling on Denying Muhammad's Prophethood
States that denying Muhammad's mission entirely or partially is kufr for the mukallaf who has received the call.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that denying any part of Muhammad's mission that is known by necessity from religion is equivalent to denying Islam itself, and the conditions under which such denial constitutes disbelief.

Core Idea
Denying the prophethood—even partially—is kufr for those who have received the call.

This passage addresses the ruling on those who reject the prophethood of Muhammad. It first refutes the notion that his message was only for Arabs. It then establishes that both total and partial denial of his mission render a person a disbeliever, provided they are legally responsible and have heard the call. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its conditions, clarifying what 'partial denial' means.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's words sink in.
The passage includes marginal notes that clarify key terms.
2
Identify the two types of denial
Find where the text mentions denying 'entirely' (kullā) and 'partially' (baʿḍan). Notice the condition attached to partial denial.
The commentary explains that partial denial applies only to what is known from religion by necessity.
3
Understand the conditions for kufr
The ruling only applies to a mukallaf (legally responsible adult) who has received the prophetic call (daʿwah). Why are these conditions important?
This safeguards against applying the ruling to those who never heard the message.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
The note says this ruling is for Ahl al-Sunna absolutely. Ask yourself: why is partial denial treated as seriously as total denial?
It preserves the integrity of necessarily known teachings of Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعموا تخصيص رسالته بالعرب ، ومَن نفى بعثته كُلًّا أو بعضا (۱) كَمَن نفى الإسلام كذلك فهو كافر عند الأشاعرة (۲) إن كان مُكلَّفا وبلغته الدعوة . (۱) قوله: (كلا) أي: إن أنكر بعثته من أصلها وقوله : (أو بعضا) أي: بشرط أن يكون معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة. الشنواني . (٢) أي عند أهل السنة مطلقا . الشنواني .
They claimed that his message is specific to the Arabs. And whoever denies his mission entirely or partially (1) is like one who denies Islam likewise, and he is a disbeliever according to the Ash'aris (2) if he is obligated (mukallaf) and the call has reached him. (1) He said: 'entirely (kullā)' means he denies his mission from its origin, and his saying 'or partially' means on condition that it is known from religion by necessity. al-Shanwānī. (2) i.e., according to Ahl al-Sunna absolutely. al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling on those who reject the prophethood of Muhammad. It begins by refuting a claim that his message was only for the Arabs. The author then states that denying his mission, whether entirely or in any part that is necessarily known from religion, is equivalent to denying Islam itself. Such a person is judged a disbeliever (kāfir) according to the Ash'aris, provided the person is a legally responsible adult (mukallaf) and has received the prophetic call. The marginal notes clarify that 'entirely' means rejecting the prophethood from its very origin, while 'partially' means rejecting some aspect that is known by necessity from religion, such as the obligation of prayer or fasting. The note also confirms that this ruling applies absolutely to Ahl al-Sunna (the Sunnis).

Correction: Partial denial of necessarily known teachings is also kufr.

Checkpoint
Under what condition is a person who denies part of Muhammad's mission considered a disbeliever?
A) If he denies any part, regardless of whether it is known by necessity
B) Only if he denies the entire prophethood
C) If he denies a part that is known from religion by necessity
D) Only if he publicly declares denial

Key Takeaway
Rejecting any part of Muhammad's prophethood that is necessarily known from religion makes a person a disbeliever, if they are legally responsible and have received the call.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If so, what does this passage reveal about the state of your imān in that moment?
?
Consider the condition that the call must have reached the person. How does this affect your responsibility toward others who may not have heard the message? Does your heart bear the weight of conveying the call clearly, or have you been negligent in sharing the truth that is necessarily known?
?
The passage declares that even partial denial is equivalent to outright rejection. Look at your own practice: are there any commands of the Prophet ﷺ that you have knowingly neglected or justified away? How does this verse challenge you to take every aspect of his sunnah and the clear teachings of Islam with absolute seriousness?

Noah's Universal Mission After the Flood: Incidental, Not Original
Explains why Noah's universality after the flood was incidental and that he was not sent to jinn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the universality of Noah's mission after the flood was an incidental consequence of the destruction of all other people, not an original feature of his prophethood, and how this distinguishes it from Muhammad's universal mission.

Core Idea
Noah's universality was a result of the flood, not a divinely ordained original commission.

This passage addresses a potential objection: if Noah was sent to all humans after the flood, does that mean his mission was universal like Muhammad's? The author explains that Noah's universality was incidental—it happened only because everyone else drowned, leaving only the ark's inhabitants. Moreover, Noah was not sent to the jinn, further limiting the scope of his mission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage about Noah's universality and its translation. Notice how the author uses the term 'اتفاقي' (accidental/incidental).
2
Distinguish incidental from original
The passage explains that Noah's universality was not part of his original commission but resulted from the flood. Compare this to Muhammad's mission, which was universal from the start.
This distinction is key to understanding the uniqueness of Muhammad's prophethood.
3
Consider the jinn point
Note that Noah was not sent to the jinn. This further limits his mission and reinforces that only Muhammad was sent to both humans and jinn.
The footnote explains why this is the best answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عموم رسالة نوح على نبينا وعليه الصلاة والسَّلام بعد الطوفان (۳) فأمر اتفاقي (٤) ؛ لأنَّه لم يَسْلُمْ مِن الهلاك إلَّا مَن كان معه في السَّفينة، على أنه لم يُرسل للجن (٥) ، وأمَّا تسخير الجن والإنس لسليمان على نبينا وعليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام فهو تسخيرُ سَلطَنَة وملك لا تسخير نبوّة. (۳) قوله : ( بعد الطوفان ) ظاهره أنها قبل الطوفان لم تكن عامة، وقيل: بل عامة ؛ وإلا لما إغراق الجميع. الأمير. (٤) هذا جواب عما قد يقال : إن تعميم البعثة ليس خاصا بنبينا بل مثله نوح، فإنه كان مبعوثا لجميع من في الأرض بعد الطوفان ، وحاصل الجواب : أن هذا العموم لم يكن في أصل بعثة ، وإنما وقع لأجل ما حدث من انحصار الخلق في الموجودين بهلاك سائر الناس، وأما نبينا صلى الله عليه وسلم فعُموم رسالته في أصل البعثة ، وذلك خاص به، وقد يقال: إن التعميم خاص بزمن نوح فقط ، فلا يساوي التعميم الذي لنبينا، فإن تعميم رسالته لكل زمان بعده ، فأين التعميم الخاص من التعميم العام ؟ على أنَّ سيدنا نوحاً لم يُرسل إلى الجن، فإنه لم يرسل لهم إلا نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وأما تسخير الجن لسليمان عليه السلام فتسخير سلطنة وملك لا تسخير نبوة. الشنواني والباجوري في التحفة . (5) قوله : ( على أنه لم يرسل للجن ) هو الأحسنُ في الجواب، لاقتضاء الأول أنَّ المغرقين لم يُرسل لهم سيدنا نوح ، فيقال : إذا كان لم يرسل لهم فما موجب غرقهم ؟ . الشنواني .
As for the universality of Noah's mission—upon our Prophet and him be prayer and peace—after the flood (3), it is an agreed-upon matter (4); because no one survived destruction except those who were with him in the ark, although he was not sent to the jinn (5). And as for the subjugation of the jinn and humans to Solomon—upon our Prophet and him be prayer and peace—it is a subjugation of sovereignty and kingship, not a subjugation of prophethood. (3) He said: 'after the flood' — its apparent meaning is that before the flood it was not universal, and it is said: rather it was universal; otherwise the drowning of all would not have occurred. al-Amīr. (4) This is an answer to what might be said: 'Universality of mission is not specific to our Prophet, but rather similar to Noah, for he was sent to all those on earth after the flood.' The summary of the answer: This universality was not in the original mission, but occurred due to the restriction of creation to those present because of the destruction of other people. As for our Prophet—peace be upon him—his universality is in the original mission, and that is specific to him. And it may be said: The universality is specific to the time of Noah only, so it is not equal to the universality of our Prophet, for the universality of his message is for every time after him. So where is the specific universality compared to the general universality? Moreover, our master Noah was not sent to the jinn; none was sent to them except our Prophet Muhammad—peace be upon him. As for the subjugation of the jinn to Solomon—peace be upon him—it is a subjugation of sovereignty and kingship, not a subjugation of prophethood. al-Shanwānī and al-Bājūrī in al-Tuḥfa. (5) He said: 'although he was not sent to the jinn' — this is the best answer, because the first implies that the drowned were not sent to by our master Noah, so it is said: if he was not sent to them, what is the cause of their drowning? al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature of Noah's prophetic mission and clarifies that it was not universal in the same way as Muhammad's. It begins by stating that the universality of Noah's mission after the flood is a matter of agreement among scholars, because after the flood, only those in the ark survived, so his message effectively became universal to all remaining humans. However, the author notes that Noah was not sent to the jinn. The passage then contrasts this with Solomon's power over jinn and humans, emphasizing that this was a form of political sovereignty (saltana) and kingship (mulk), not a prophetic function. The marginal notes expand on these points: the first note clarifies that before the flood, Noah's mission may not have been universal; the second note explains that this universality after the flood was a contingent result of the destruction of others, whereas Muhammad's universality was inherent in his original mission. The note further distinguishes that Noah's universality was limited to his own time, while Muhammad's message is for all subsequent times. Finally, the best answer for why Noah was not sent to the jinn is that if he had been, then those jinn who were drowned would have been punished without a prophet being sent to them, which would be problematic.

Key Terms
عموم رسالة
اتفاقي
جن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Noah's mission was universal in the same way as Muhammad's.
Correction: Noah's universality was incidental due to the flood and not original; also he was not sent to jinn.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Noah's universal mission after the flood described as 'اتفاقي' (incidental)?
A) Because it was a coincidence
B) Because it happened only after the flood due to the destruction of all other people
C) Because it was not a real mission
D) Because it was for the jinn only

Key Takeaway
Noah's universal mission after the flood was an accidental result of the destruction of all other people, not an original feature of his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though Muhammad's message is for every person you meet, or do you unconsciously limit it to those like you? What would change if you acted on its universal scope today?
?
If Noah's universality was merely accidental, what does that imply about the deliberate design of Muhammad's prophethood? How does that affect your confidence in Islam's finality?
?
Are there areas of your life where you treat your faith as a cultural inheritance rather than a universal call? What one step can you take this week to break that pattern?

Solomon's Subjugation of Jinn and Humans: Sovereignty, Not Prophethood
Clarifies that Solomon's subjugation of jinn and humans was kingship, not prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Solomon's control over jinn and humans was a form of political sovereignty (saltana) and kingship (mulk), not a prophetic miracle, and why this distinction is important in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Solomon's power over jinn and humans was kingly authority, not a sign of prophethood.

This passage clarifies the nature of Solomon's miraculous subjugation of the jinn and humans. The author distinguishes between two types of subjugation: one that is a sign of prophethood, and another that is a form of kingship. Solomon's case falls into the latter category, preserving the distinction between prophetic miracles and kingly power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about Solomon
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Note the key terms: تسخير, سلطنة, ملك, نبوة.
2
Understand the distinction
The passage explicitly states that Solomon's subjugation is not that of prophethood but of sovereignty and kingship. Reflect on what this means for the nature of his miracles.
Solomon was both a prophet and a king, but his power over jinn is attributed to his kingship.
3
Apply the distinction
Think about why this distinction matters. It maintains the uniqueness of prophetic miracles and prevents confusion between the functions of prophethood and worldly rule.
This is part of a broader theological discussion about the types of miracles (karamat vs. mu'jizat).

?

?
Solomon had both prophethood and kingship, but you may only have one. If you are given worldly authority, how will you ensure you do not confuse it with spiritual rank? What concrete step will you take today to keep yourself from treating power as a sign of divine favor?
?
The subjugation of jinn was a kingly tool, not a prophetic miracle. In your own life, do you ever mistake worldly success (wealth, status, control) as proof of spiritual achievement? How will you remind yourself of the distinction starting now?

The Muhammadan Law Abrogates All Others
Declares Muhammadan law abrogates others and cannot be abrogated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Muhammadan law abrogates all previous laws and cannot be abrogated by any other law, though abrogation of some rulings within the same law is permissible.

Core Idea
The Muhammadan law is final and abrogates all prior laws.

In this passage, the author establishes the supremacy of the Muhammadan law. It declares that the law brought by Prophet Muhammad abrogates all previous divine laws and cannot itself be abrogated. However, within this law, some rulings may be abrogated by others, as explicitly stated.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation.
Read the Arabic text carefully, then the translation to grasp the full meaning.
2
Understand the claim of abrogation.
Notice that the Muhammadan law is described as nāsikh (abrogating) of all other laws, and that it cannot be abrogated by any other law.
This is based on the sealing of prophethood and universality of the mission.
3
Identify the exception.
The passage explicitly allows internal abrogation (naskh) of some rulings by others within the same law.
This is indicated by the author's verse 'wa-naskh ba'di shar'ihi bi-l-ba'di ajiz.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشريعة المحمدية ناسخة لغيرها من الشرائع . ثمَّ ذَكرَ ما يترتَّبُ على ختم النُّبوَّة به وعموم بعثته بقوله: (فَشَرْعُهُ لَا يُنْسَخُ بِغَيْرِهِ) أي: فيتفرع على ما ذُكر أنَّ دينه وما جاءَ به عن الله من الأحكام، قرآنيَّةً كانتْ أو سُنّيّةٌ ، كلا أو بعضا، لا يُرفع بشرع غيره، لا كُلًّا ولا بعضاً ، وأما نسخُ بعض أحكام شرعه بالبعض الآخرِ فهو ما يُصرّح به في قوله: (وَنَسْخُ بَعْضِ شَرْعِهِ بِالبَعْضِ أَجِزْ) . والشَّرْعُ لغةً: البيانُ، واصطلاحًا تجويز الشيء أو تحريمه، أي: جعله جائزا أو حراماً . والشَّارِعُ : مُبيِّنُ الأحكام ، والشَّريعة: الطَّريقة في الدين. والمَشرُوع: ما أظهره الشَّرع. (۱) قوله: (تجويز الشيء) في العبارة قصور ، وكان الأولى أن يزيد الكراهة والوجوب والندب ، فالشرع عبارة عن الأحكام الشرعية ، إلا أن يُقال: المراد بالتجويز ما قابل التحريم، فيشمل الإيجاب والندب والكراهة والإباحة ، والأحسنُ أن يراد به النّسب التامة ، كثبوت الوجوب للصلاة. الشنواني . (۲) قوله : (جائزا) أي غير حرام فيشمل المندوب والمكروه والواجب. الأمير. (۳) قوله : ( مُبيِّنُ الأحكام) حقيقة هو الله ، ويُطلق مجازا عليه . الشنواني . (٤) أي أحكام أظهرها الشرعُ ، مع أنَّ الشرع عين الأحكام ، فيقتضي أن الشرع أظهر نفسه، وأجيب: بأن المراد بالشرع ثانيا الشارع ، فالمشروع والشريعة على هذا شيء واحد . الشنواني .
The Muhammadan law is abrogating (nāsikh) of other laws. Then he mentioned what follows from the sealing of prophethood with him and the universality of his mission, saying: 'So his law is not abrogated by any other.' That is, it branches from what has been mentioned that his religion and what he brought from God of rulings, whether Qur'anic or Sunnaic, entirely or partially, cannot be lifted by another law, neither entirely nor partially. As for abrogating some rulings of his law by other parts of it, that is explicitly stated in his saying: 'And allow the abrogation of some of his law by some of it.' And al-shar' linguistically means clarification (bayān), and terminologically means permitting a thing (tajwīz al-shay') or forbidding it (taḥrīmuhu), i.e., making it permissible or forbidden. And al-shāri' (the Lawgiver) is the one who clarifies the rulings. And al-sharī'ah is the method in religion. And al-mashrū' is what the shar' has manifested. (1) He said: 'permitting a thing' — the expression is deficient; it would have been better to add dislike (karāha), obligation (wujūb), and recommendation (nadb). For al-shar' is an expression for the legal rulings. Unless it is said that what is meant by 'permitting' is what is contrasted with forbidding, thus including obligation, recommendation, dislike, and permissibility. And the best is that it refers to complete relations, like the establishment of obligation for prayer. al-Shanwānī. (2) He said: 'permissible' means not forbidden, thus including recommended, disliked, and obligatory. al-Amīr. (3) He said: 'the one who clarifies the rulings' — in reality it is Allah, and it is applied to him metaphorically. al-Shanwānī. (4) i.e., rulings that the shar' has manifested, although the shar' is identical to the rulings, so it would imply that the shar' manifested itself. And it is answered: what is meant by 'shar'' secondly is the Lawgiver (shāri'), so al-mashrū' and al-sharī'ah on this are one thing. al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage establishes the finality and supremacy of the Muhammadan law. It begins by declaring that the Muhammadan law abrogates (nāsikh) all previous laws. Then it explains that because prophethood is sealed with Muhammad and his mission is universal, his law cannot be abrogated by any other law—neither entirely nor partially. However, internal abrogation (naskh) of some rulings by others within the same law is permissible, as indicated in the author's verse. The passage then defines key terms: 'shar'' linguistically means 'clarification' (bayān), and technically means 'making a thing permissible or forbidden' (tajwīz or taḥrīm). 'Shāri'' is the one who clarifies rulings, and in reality it is Allah, though metaphorically applied. 'Sharī'ah' is the path in religion, and 'mashrū'' is what the shar' has manifested. The marginal notes critique the definition of shar', suggesting that the term 'permitting' (tajwīz) is incomplete without including other categories like obligation, recommendation, and dislike. A better interpretation is that 'permitting' is contrasted with 'forbidding', thus encompassing all non-prohibited categories. The note also clarifies that 'jā'iz' (permissible) in the text means 'not forbidden', so it includes recommended, disliked, and obligatory acts. Finally, the note addresses the apparent circularity in defining mashrū' as 'what the shar' has manifested' when shar' is itself the rulings, and resolves it by taking 'shar'' in the second occurrence to mean the Lawgiver (shāri'), making mashrū' and sharī'ah identical.

Key Terms
ناسخ
منسوخ
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Muhammadan law can be abrogated by a later divine law, like a future prophet.
Correction: The passage clearly states that his law cannot be abrogated by any other, indicating the finality of prophethood and the supremacy of his law.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the status of the Muhammadan law regarding other laws?
A) It is abrogated by later laws
B) It abrogates all previous laws and cannot be abrogated
C) It can be abrogated partially
D) It is equal to other laws

Key Takeaway
The Muhammadan law abrogates all prior laws and cannot be abrogated by any later law, though internal abrogation of rulings is allowed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have there been moments when you wished to follow a ruling from another religion or ideology that contradicts the Muhammadan law? How does this lesson demand that you respond to such desires?
?
If you truly believe that the Muhammadan law is final and cannot be abrogated, what practical change must you make in how you approach modern issues not explicitly mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah?
?
When you encounter a difficult ruling in Islam, do you ever doubt its applicability or completeness? After this lesson, what commitment are you willing to make regarding your trust in the Sharia's finality?

Definition of Shar' and Related Terms
Defines shar' and related terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical meaning of 'shar'' and its related terms 'shāri'', 'sharī'ah', and 'mashrū''.

Core Idea
Shar' means permitting or forbidding, and its derivatives clarify the lawgiver and the legislated path.

After establishing the supremacy of the Muhammadan law, the author defines the fundamental term 'shar''. He gives its linguistic meaning as 'clarification' and its technical meaning as 'permitting or forbidding a thing'. The marginal notes refine this definition and explain the related terms 'shāri'', 'sharī'ah', and 'mashrū''.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of shar' in Arabic.
Read the linguistic and technical definitions of the term 'shar'.
Note that the linguistic meaning is 'bayān' (clarification).
2
Understand the technical meaning and its critique.
The technical definition is 'tajwīz al-shay' aw taḥrīmuhu' (permitting or forbidding a thing). The marginal notes point out that this is incomplete and should include other categories like obligation and recommendation.
A better interpretation is that 'permitting' contrasts with 'forbidding', covering all non-prohibited rulings.
3
Learn the derivatives.
The passage defines shāri' as the one who clarifies rulings (Allah), sharī'ah as the path in religion, and mashrū' as what the shar' has manifested, with a note resolving a circularity.

الشريعة المحمدية ناسخة لغيرها من الشرائع . ثمَّ ذَكرَ ما يترتَّبُ على ختم النُّبوَّة به وعموم بعثته بقوله: (فَشَرْعُهُ لَا يُنْسَخُ بِغَيْرِهِ) أي: فيتفرع على ما ذُكر أنَّ دينه وما جاءَ به عن الله من الأحكام، قرآنيَّةً كانتْ أو سُنّيّةٌ ، كلا أو بعضا، لا يُرفع بشرع غيره، لا كُلًّا ولا بعضاً ، وأما نسخُ بعض أحكام شرعه بالبعض الآخرِ فهو ما يُصرّح به في قوله: (وَنَسْخُ بَعْضِ شَرْعِهِ بِالبَعْضِ أَجِزْ) . والشَّرْعُ لغةً: البيانُ، واصطلاحًا تجويز الشيء أو تحريمه، أي: جعله جائزا أو حراماً . والشَّارِعُ : مُبيِّنُ الأحكام ، والشَّريعة: الطَّريقة في الدين. والمَشرُوع: ما أظهره الشَّرع. (۱) قوله: (تجويز الشيء) في العبارة قصور ، وكان الأولى أن يزيد الكراهة والوجوب والندب ، فالشرع عبارة عن الأحكام الشرعية ، إلا أن يُقال: المراد بالتجويز ما قابل التحريم، فيشمل الإيجاب والندب والكراهة والإباحة ، والأحسنُ أن يراد به النّسب التامة ، كثبوت الوجوب للصلاة. الشنواني . (۲) قوله : (جائزا) أي غير حرام فيشمل المندوب والمكروه والواجب. الأمير. (۳) قوله : ( مُبيِّنُ الأحكام) حقيقة هو الله ، ويُطلق مجازا عليه . الشنواني . (٤) أي أحكام أظهرها الشرعُ ، مع أنَّ الشرع عين الأحكام ، فيقتضي أن الشرع أظهر نفسه، وأجيب: بأن المراد بالشرع ثانيا الشارع ، فالمشروع والشريعة على هذا شيء واحد . الشنواني .
The Muhammadan law is abrogating (nāsikh) of other laws. Then he mentioned what follows from the sealing of prophethood with him and the universality of his mission, saying: 'So his law is not abrogated by any other.' That is, it branches from what has been mentioned that his religion and what he brought from God of rulings, whether Qur'anic or Sunnaic, entirely or partially, cannot be lifted by another law, neither entirely nor partially. As for abrogating some rulings of his law by other parts of it, that is explicitly stated in his saying: 'And allow the abrogation of some of his law by some of it.' And al-shar' linguistically means clarification (bayān), and terminologically means permitting a thing (tajwīz al-shay') or forbidding it (taḥrīmuhu), i.e., making it permissible or forbidden. And al-shāri' (the Lawgiver) is the one who clarifies the rulings. And al-sharī'ah is the method in religion. And al-mashrū' is what the shar' has manifested. (1) He said: 'permitting a thing' — the expression is deficient; it would have been better to add dislike (karāha), obligation (wujūb), and recommendation (nadb). For al-shar' is an expression for the legal rulings. Unless it is said that what is meant by 'permitting' is what is contrasted with forbidding, thus including obligation, recommendation, dislike, and permissibility. And the best is that it refers to complete relations, like the establishment of obligation for prayer. al-Shanwānī. (2) He said: 'permissible' means not forbidden, thus including recommended, disliked, and obligatory. al-Amīr. (3) He said: 'the one who clarifies the rulings' — in reality it is Allah, and it is applied to him metaphorically. al-Shanwānī. (4) i.e., rulings that the shar' has manifested, although the shar' is identical to the rulings, so it would imply that the shar' manifested itself. And it is answered: what is meant by 'shar'' secondly is the Lawgiver (shāri'), so al-mashrū' and al-sharī'ah on this are one thing. al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage establishes the finality and supremacy of the Muhammadan law. It begins by declaring that the Muhammadan law abrogates (nāsikh) all previous laws. Then it explains that because prophethood is sealed with Muhammad and his mission is universal, his law cannot be abrogated by any other law—neither entirely nor partially. However, internal abrogation (naskh) of some rulings by others within the same law is permissible, as indicated in the author's verse. The passage then defines key terms: 'shar'' linguistically means 'clarification' (bayān), and technically means 'making a thing permissible or forbidden' (tajwīz or taḥrīm). 'Shāri'' is the one who clarifies rulings, and in reality it is Allah, though metaphorically applied. 'Sharī'ah' is the path in religion, and 'mashrū'' is what the shar' has manifested. The marginal notes critique the definition of shar', suggesting that the term 'permitting' (tajwīz) is incomplete without including other categories like obligation, recommendation, and dislike. A better interpretation is that 'permitting' is contrasted with 'forbidding', thus encompassing all non-prohibited categories. The note also clarifies that 'jā'iz' (permissible) in the text means 'not forbidden', so it includes recommended, disliked, and obligatory acts. Finally, the note addresses the apparent circularity in defining mashrū' as 'what the shar' has manifested' when shar' is itself the rulings, and resolves it by taking 'shar'' in the second occurrence to mean the Lawgiver (shāri'), making mashrū' and sharī'ah identical.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the technical definition of shar' only covers permissibility and prohibition, excluding other categories like obligation.
Correction: The commentary clarifies that 'permitting' contrasts with 'forbidding' and thus includes obligation, recommendation, dislike, and permissibility.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Abrogation Status of the Muhammadan Sharia
The author states the abrogation ruling of the Sharia of Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique status of the Muhammadan Sharia regarding abrogation: it supersedes all previous laws, cannot be superseded by any later law, yet internal abrogation remains permissible.

Core Idea
The Muhammadan Sharia is the final law, abrogating all before, abrogated by none, but internally flexible.

This passage from the text establishes a key principle of Islamic theology: the Sharia brought by Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) holds a unique position in salvation history. It abrogates previous divine laws but is itself final and unchangeable from outside. However, within its own framework, some rulings may be replaced by others as needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice the three distinct claims made about the Sharia.
The passage uses the term 'sharia' broadly to mean the entire religious law.
2
Identify the three claims
Find where the passage states that the Muhammadan Sharia abrogates others, that it cannot be abrogated by another, and that internal abrogation is allowed.
Each claim is separated by a distinct phrase.
3
Understand why it is not abrogated
The passage links the non-abrogability to the sealing of prophethood and the universality of Muhammad's mission. Reflect on why finality implies no later sharia can supersede it.
4
Distinguish external vs internal abrogation
Note that 'not abrogated by another' refers to external abrogation by a different sharia, while 'abrogation of some of his Sharia by some' refers to internal revision within the same Sharia.
Internal abrogation is a process that occurred during the Prophet's lifetime through revelations superseding earlier ones.

The Muhammadan Sharia abrogates other sharias. Then he mentioned what follows from the sealing of prophethood with him and the universality of his mission in his statement: (His Sharia is not abrogated by another), meaning: it branches from what was mentioned that his religion and what he brought from Allah of rulings, whether Quranic or Sunnaic, in whole or in part, are not lifted by another sharia, neither wholly nor partially. As for the abrogation of some of his Sharia's rulings by others, it is that which is expressed in his statement: (and the abrogation of some of his Sharia by some permit).
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the ruling regarding the Sharia of Muhammad (peace be upon him) in relation to abrogation. It begins by affirming that the Muhammadan Sharia abrogates all previous sharias, meaning it supersedes them. Then the author explains what follows from the fact that Muhammad is the seal of the prophets and his mission is universal: his Sharia cannot be abrogated by any other sharia, neither in whole nor in part. However, the abrogation of some of its rulings by other rulings within the same Sharia is permitted, as expressed in the line (and the abrogation of some of his Sharia by some permit). Thus the passage establishes both the non-abrogability of the Sharia from without and the possibility of internal abrogation.

Definition of Naskh and Proof of the Sharia's Perpetuity
The author defines naskh and proves the Sharia's perpetuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of naskh (abrogation) as the raising of a legal ruling by a legal proof, and why the Sharia of Muhammad ﷺ is perpetual until the end of time.

Core Idea
The Sharia of Islam is the final, unchangeable divine law until the Day of Judgment.

In this passage, the author defines naskh and then establishes that the Sharia of Muhammad cannot be abrogated. Understanding this concept is crucial for appreciating the finality of prophethood and the completeness of Islam. Let's dive into the definition and the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the author first defines naskh linguistically and terminologically before moving to the perpetuity of the Sharia.
2
Understand the two definitions of naskh
Identify the linguistic definition (removal and transfer) and the terminological definition (raising a legal ruling by a legal proof). Ask yourself: why is the terminological definition important for theology?
The ruling itself is not erased, only its applicability is lifted.
3
Note the statement of perpetuity
The author asserts that the Sharia of Muhammad is continuous until the end of time. Find the phrase 'until the time is abrogated' and understand what it means.
It means until the world ends and the Resurrection comes.
4
Examine the reasons and evidence
The author gives a rational reason (inconceivability of a later abrogating sharia) and scriptural evidence from the Quran and hadith. List the two Quranic verses and the hadith cited.
The verses emphasize that Islam is the only accepted religion.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this passage is foundational: it affirms the finality of prophethood and the completeness of the Islamic message. How does this affect a Muslim's understanding of religious law?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنسخ لغة: الإزالة والنقل، واصطلاحا: رفع حكم شرعي بدليل شرعي. فشرع نبينا صلى الله عليه وسلم مُستمرٌ حتى الزَّمَانُ يُنْسَخُ أي: حتى يَنقَضِي الزَّمانُ ويزول بحضور القيامة، لعدم تصور الآتي بما يكون به النَّسخُ، وعدم قَبُولِ زمانٍ مِن الأزمنة المُستقبلة لوقوع ذلكَ فيه؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الدِّينَ عِندَ اللَّهِ الْإِسْلَمُ﴾ [آل عمران: 19] ﴿وَمَن يَبْتَغِ غَيْرَ الْإِسْلَامِ دِينًا فَلَن يُقْبَلَ مِنْهُ﴾ [آل عمران: 85] ولقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لا تزال هذه الأمة قائمة على أمر الله تعالى»
And al-naskh (abrogation) linguistically: removal and transfer, and terminologically: the raising of a legal ruling by a legal proof. So the Sharia of our Prophet, peace be upon him, is continuous until the time is abrogated, i.e., until time passes away and ceases with the advent of the Resurrection, because it is inconceivable that something later comes by which abrogation occurs, and because no future time can accept its occurrence due to His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam' (Al 'Imran 19) and 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' (Al 'Imran 85), and due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'This ummah will not cease to be steadfast upon the command of Allah.'
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage defines naskh (abrogation) and then proves the perpetuity of the Sharia of Muhammad. It begins with a definition: linguistically naskh means 'removal and transfer'; terminologically it is 'the raising of a legal ruling by a legal proof' (meaning the removal of a ruling's applicability, not the ruling itself which is eternal). The passage then asserts that the Sharia of Muhammad is continuous until 'the time is abrogated', i.e., until the end of the world and the Day of Resurrection. This is because it is impossible to conceive of a later sharia that would abrogate it, and no future time can accommodate such an abrogation. The proof is from two Quranic verses: 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam' and 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him', which imply that Islam is the final and only accepted religion. Also, a hadith states that this ummah will remain steadfast upon Allah's command until the end of time, indicating no abrogation of the Sharia.

Key Terms

حكم شرعي
دليل شرعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Naskh removes the ruling itself permanently.
Correction: Naskh removes the applicability of a ruling, not the ruling's existence; the ruling remains but its implementation is lifted.

Checkpoint

C) Because the Sharia is too perfect.
D) Because abrogation is not possible in any religion.

Key Takeaway
Naskh means lifting a legal ruling by a legal proof, and the Sharia of Islam is final and unchangeable until the Day of Judgment.

The Necessity of Abrogating Previous Scriptures
The commentator affirms the necessary abrogation of prior revelations and refutes Jewish and Christian objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the abrogation of previous scriptures by Islam is a necessary doctrine supported by the Quran, hadith tawatur, and Muslim consensus.

Core Idea
Abrogation of previous laws by Islam is rationally possible and scripturally necessary.

The commentator explains a line from the poem declaring that the abrogation of earlier sharias by the sharia of Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) occurred necessarily. This lesson unpacks the evidence he presents—the Quran, mass-transmitted hadith, and consensus—and the refutation of those who deny it. You will see how the commentator combines rational possibility with revealed certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic poem line and commentary once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the emphatic phrase 'وقعت حتما' (occurred necessarily).
2
Identify the three proofs
The commentator provides three evidences: a Quranic verse (Al-Imran:85), numerous hadith reaching tawatur, and the consensus of Muslims. Locate each one in the passage.
These correspond to the three primary sources of Islamic law.
3
Understand 'occurred necessarily'
The phrase 'وقعت حتما' means the abrogation is decisive and not open to interpretation. Why does the commentator insist on necessity? Reflect on the consequences of denying it.
Denying abrogation leads to denying prophethood, as the commentator states.
4
Reflect on the supplication
The poem ends with a supplication against those who deny abrogation. Consider why such a strong response is warranted: it protects the foundation of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَنَسْخُهُ لِشَرْعِ غَيْرِهِ وَقَعْ حَتْمًا أَذَلَّ اللهُ مَنْ لَهُ مَنَعْ
ثمَّ أشار إلى الرَّدِّ على اليهودِ والنَّصارى ومَن جرى مجراهم، حيث زعموا أن شرع نبينا لم يَنْسَخُ شرعَ أحدٍ مِن الأنبياء، بقوله: (وَنَسْخُهُ) الله :أي: شرع نبينا محمد ( لِشَرْع) كل نبي (غَيْرِه) صلى الله عليه وسلم (وَقَعْ حَتْمًا) أي : مُتَحتّما لا يقبلُ التَّأويل ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَبْتَغِ غَيْرَ الْإِسْلَامِ دِينًا ﴾ [آل عمران: ٨٥] الآية. والأحاديثُ في ذلك كثيرةٌ بلغت جملتها مَبْلغَ التَّواتُر، ومراده رحمه الله تعالى: أنَّ النسخ جائز عقلا واقع سمعا، بإجماع المسلين، فلذلك دعا على مَن مَنعهُ بقوله: (أَذَلَّ اللهُ مَنْ لَهُ مَنَعْ) أي: أَلحقِّ الذُّلَّ ونفى أنواع العِزّ عن اللذين منعوا نسخ شرع نبينا الشرع غيره؛ توسلا للقول بنفي نبوّته
His abrogation of the sharia of others occurred necessarily; Allah has disgraced the one who denies it. Then he pointed to the refutation of the Jews and Christians and those who follow their way, since they claimed that the sharia of our Prophet did not abrogate the sharia of any of the prophets, by his saying: 'And its abrogation' i.e., the sharia of our Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) 'of the sharia of every prophet other than him' 'occurred necessarily' i.e., decisively not open to interpretation, because of His saying: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam...' [Al-Imran:85] and the hadiths on this are numerous, their total reaching the level of tawatur. And his intention (may Allah have mercy on him) is that abrogation is rationally permissible and has occurred according to revelation, by consensus of the Muslims. Therefore he supplicated against the one who denied it by his saying: 'Allah has disgraced the one who denies it' i.e., He inflicted disgrace and removed all honor from those who denied the abrogation of our Prophet's sharia to other sharias, as a means to deny his prophethood.

Commentary
This passage is a proof and a refutation. The commentator explains the poem line stating that the abrogation of previous scriptures by Islam is necessary and actually occurred. He then provides three evidences: the Quranic verse from Al-Imran, the numerous hadiths that reach the level of tawatur (mass transmission), and the consensus of Muslims. He clarifies that this abrogation is rationally possible and has been reported in revelation. Finally, he explains the supplication in the poem against those who deny this, as it leads to denying the prophethood of Muhammad.

The Permissibility of Abrogation Within Islamic Law
Commentator clarifies the permissibility of internal abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that abrogation (naskh) of some Islamic rulings by others is permissible within the Sharia, and that this permissibility extends even to fundamental obligations like knowing God.

Core Idea
Islamic law can partially abrogate itself; permissibility, not necessity, is the rule.

The commentator unpacks a line from the poem that states 'His sharia is not abrogated by other than it.' This might seem to suggest that no abrogation is possible at all. But here he clarifies that abrogation within the Sharia—one part replacing another—is indeed permissible. This lesson will help you see the precise scope of naskh and why it is a matter of belief in possibility, not obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage aloud or silently. Notice the key phrase 'أَجِزْ' (permit) which is a command to believe in the permissibility of this type of abrogation.
The passage is from a commentary on a theological poem, so it explains a prior line.
2
Understand the translation
Compare the Arabic with the English translation. Focus on the phrase 'by some' vs. 'by other than it'—the distinction is crucial. The Sharia is not replaced by a different law, but parts can replace other parts.
3
Grasp the key doctrinal point
The commentator emphasizes that even obligations like knowing God and prohibitions like disbelief can theoretically be abrogated. This shows the sweeping scope of naskh within the Sharia, though historically such abrogation did not occur for these core beliefs.
This guards against the misconception that abrogation only applies to minor rulings.
4
Test your understanding
Try the checkpoint question to ensure you can distinguish between 'permissible' and 'necessary' or 'impossible' regarding naskh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ شَرعَ في بيانِ مفهوم قوله: «فَشَرْعُهُ لَا يُنْسَخُ بِغَيْرِهِ» فقال: (وَنَسْخَ) أي: ووقوع نسخ (بَعْضِ ) أحكام (شَرْعِهِ ) له بالبَعْض) أي: بأحكام بعض شرعه الآخر (أَجِزْ) أي: اعتقد جواز الوقوع، واحْكُم : وشَمِل البعض المنسوخ وجوبَ معرفته سبحانه، وتحريم الكفر كما

Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a rule. The commentator clarifies that the earlier statement 'His sharia is not abrogated by other than it' means that the entire Islamic law cannot be replaced by another, but parts can be abrogated by other parts. The word 'أَجِزْ' is a command to believe in the permissibility of such abrogation. The commentator then notes that the abrogated rulings include even fundamental obligations like knowing God and prohibitions like disbelief, indicating the theoretical scope of abrogation within the sharia.

Correction: This passage shows that abrogation could theoretically touch even the most fundamental obligations and prohibitions, though in practice such abrogation did not occur.

Key Takeaway
Abrogation of some rulings by others within the Islamic Sharia is permissible, and it is a matter of belief to affirm this possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If Allah can abrogate even the prohibition of disbelief (hypothetically), then nothing is beyond His authority. Do you live with the reality that every command you follow is contingent on His will—or do you secretly assume that certain rules are permanent because they suit your comfort?

Definition of Naskh and Its Ruling
Defines naskh linguistically and terminologically and states its doctrinal rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of naskh (abrogation) according to Ahl al-Haqq, the consensus that universal abrogation has not occurred, and the susceptibility of every legal ruling, including Quranic, to abrogation.

Core Idea
Naskh: every ruling can be abrogated, but universal abrogation never occurred by consensus.

This passage from the matn defines naskh both linguistically and terminologically, then clarifies the doctrinal rule held by Ahl al-Haqq. It establishes a key distinction: although each individual legal ruling is potentially subject to abrogation, there is a consensus that the abrogation of all rulings simultaneously has not happened. The text also addresses a minority view that excludes Quranic rulings from abrogation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic source passage once, then its literal translation. Pay attention to the parentheses and numbers.
The passage is concise; read it slowly.
2
Identify the definition of naskh
The passage begins with 'تعريف النسخ لغة واصطلاحا' – the definition of naskh linguistically and terminologically. Note that it then states that this is the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq.
3
Understand the consensus point
The phrase 'ومفهومه عدم وقوع نسخ الجميع الصحيح إجماعاً' means the meaning (of this doctrine) is that non-occurrence of the abrogation of all rulings is correct by consensus. Recognize this as a communal agreement.
This consensus is a cornerstone of Sunni theology.
4
Grasp the susceptibility of rulings
The text continues: 'وإن كان كل حكم شرعي قابلا للنسخ كلا أو بعضا على المختار' – every legal ruling is susceptible to abrogation entirely or partially according to the chosen view. This includes even Quranic rulings ('شمل البعض القرآني أيضا').
Contrast this with the view of Abu Muslim al-Isfahani who denies Quranic abrogation.
5
Contrast with the opposed view
The passage ends with 'خلافا لمن منعه كأبي مسلم الأصفهاني' – contrary to those who prevent it, like Abu Muslim al-Isfahani. Understand that this is a rejection of the position that Quranic rulings are immune to abrogation.
This shows that the chosen view affirms abrogation of Quranic rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النسخ لغة واصطلاحا (۲) وهو مذهب أهل الحق (۱) ومفهومه عدم وقوع نسخ الجميع الصحيح إجماعاً وإن كان كل حكم شرعي قابلا للنسخ كلا أو بعضا على المختار وشمل البعض القرآني أيضا خلافا لمن منعه كأبي مسلم الأصفهاني
The definition of naskh (abrogation) linguistically and terminologically (2): it is the doctrine of the people of truth (1), and its meaning is the non-occurrence of abrogation of all [rulings] is correct by consensus, although every legal ruling is susceptible to abrogation entirely or partially according to the chosen view, and it includes the Quranic part as well, contrary to those who prevent it, such as Abu Muslim al-Isfahani.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage defines naskh both linguistically and terminologically and clarifies its doctrinal rule according to Ahl al-Haqq. It begins by stating that naskh is the doctrine of the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq). Then it explains the key concept: it is agreed by consensus (ijma') that abrogation of all rulings simultaneously has not occurred. However, every single legal ruling is potentially subject to abrogation, either wholly or partially, which is the preferred (mukhtar) position. This includes even Quranic rulings, contrary to the view of those who deny this, such as Abu Muslim al-Isfahani. Thus the passage distinguishes between the possibility of abrogation for each ruling and the non-occurrence of universal abrogation.

Key Terms

إجماع (ijma')

أبو مسلم الأصفهاني (Abu Muslim al-Isfahani)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that abrogation of all rulings has occurred, or that Quranic rulings cannot be abrogated at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that universal abrogation has not occurred by consensus, but every ruling, including Quranic, is susceptible to abrogation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Naskh is the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq: universal abrogation never happened by consensus, yet every legal ruling (including Quranic) is individually susceptible to abrogation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting Objections to Abrogation of the Quran by Ahad Reports
Refutes two objections to abrogation of Quran by ahad reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend the permissibility of abrogating a definitive Quranic verse with a solitary (ahad) report.

Core Idea
Definitive text can be abrogated by speculative reports; abrogation is not falsehood.

The possibility of abrogating Quranic verses with solitary reports was contested. Opponents argued that the Quran's definitive nature prevents any abrogation by non-definitive means, and that partial abrogation would imply falsehood. This lesson presents two objections and their refutations, clarifying the nuanced reasoning behind the permissibility of such abrogation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first objection
The opponent argues that since the Quran is definitive (qat'i), it cannot be abrogated by ahad (solitary) reports. Notice the initial answer and then the refined position.
The refined answer is that there is no inherent obstacle to such abrogation.
2
Examine the second objection
The opponent quotes the verse 'Falsehood cannot approach it...' to argue that partial abrogation would introduce falsehood. See how the answer distinguishes between abrogation and falsehood.
The pronoun in the verse refers to the whole Quran, and abrogation is not falsehood.
3
Grasp the key distinctions
Note the distinction between textual definitiveness (matn) and implicational definitiveness (dalala), and between abrogation (naskh) and falsehood (batil).
These distinctions are crucial for understanding the permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك المانع بأنَّ القرآن قطعي فلا يُنسخُ بآحادٍ وأجيب بأنَّ القطعي متنه لا دلالته، لكن أنت خبير بأنَّ الدلالة قد تكون قطعيةً كآية الاستقبال، فالحق أن يُقال: لا مانع من نسخه بالآحاد. وتمسك المانع أيضا بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَأْتِيهِ الْبَطِلُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَلَا مِنْ خَلْفِهِ﴾ فلو نسخ بعضه لتطرق إليه البطلان، وأجيب بأنَّ الضمير لمجموع القرآن، وهو لا ينسخ إجماعاً، ولأنَّ النسخ إبطال أو رفع للحكم لا باطل، فإن الباطل ضد الحق.
The opponent adhered to the argument that the Qur'an is definitive (qat'i) so it cannot be abrogated by ahad (solitary reports). It was answered that the definitiveness is in its text (matn), not its implication (dalala). But you are aware that implication may be definitive, like the verse of the qibla. So the truth is to say: there is no obstacle to its abrogation by ahad. The opponent also adhered to His statement, the Exalted: 'Falsehood cannot approach it from before it or from behind it.' So if some of it were abrogated, falsehood would enter it. It was answered that the pronoun refers to the entirety of the Qur'an, and it is not abrogated by consensus, and because abrogation is nullification or lifting of a ruling, not falsehood, for falsehood is the opposite of truth.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage presents two objections from the one who denies abrogation of the Qur'an by solitary (ahad) reports, along with their refutations. The first objection: The Qur'an is definitive (qat'i) in transmission, so it cannot be abrogated by ahad reports which are speculative. The initial answer: The definitiveness applies to the text (matn) itself, not to its implication (dalala). However, the commentator notes that implication can also be definitive, as in the case of the qibla verse, so the better answer is simply that there is no inherent obstacle to such abrogation. The second objection: The verse 'Falsehood cannot approach it from before it or from behind it' (41:42) implies that partial abrogation would introduce falsehood. The answer: The pronoun refers to the entire Qur'an, which collectively is not abrogated by consensus; moreover, abrogation is not falsehood—it is the lifting or nullification of a ruling, which is a legitimate act, whereas falsehood is the opposite of truth.

Key Terms
قطع (qat')
آحاد (ahad)
متن (matn)
دلالة (dalala)
باطل (batil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definitive nature of the Quran's text prevents any abrogation by speculative reports.
Correction: The text (matn) is definitive, but its implication (dalala) may be speculative; moreover, even if implication is definitive, there is no inherent obstacle to abrogation by ahad. Abrogation is not falsehood.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection of the opponent regarding abrogation of the Quran?
A) That the Quran is definitive and cannot be abrogated.
B) That partial abrogation would allow falsehood to enter the Quran.
C) That abrogation must be from Quran to Quran only.
D) That Abu Muslim al-Isfahani is the author of the objection.

Key Takeaway
Though the Quran's text is definitive, its verses can be abrogated by ahad reports, and such abrogation is not falsehood but a legitimate lifting of a ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you encounter a ruling from the Quran that you know has been abrogated by a hadith, do you accept it intellectually or does a part of you feel uneasy that a 'lesser' source changes Allah's word? Examine your heart and ask: do I truly submit to Allah's wisdom in abrogation? Commit to studying the wisdom behind naskh.

The passage says abrogation is not falsehood but lifting of a ruling. Is there an area of your life where you struggle to accept that Allah's commands may change (e.g., in different times or circumstances) because you fear losing certainty? Reflect on a specific ruling you find difficult and ask: will I trust Allah's legislative wisdom even when it challenges my sense of permanence?
?
If you have ever preferred a personal opinion over a clear proof because you thought it 'contradicted' the Quran, what does this passage teach you about the nature of contradiction and falsehood? Will you now differentiate between legitimate abrogation and your own preferences?

The Four Types of Abrogation
Classifies the four types of naskh with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of naskh (abrogation) in Islamic law and their examples from the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
The four types of naskh show how divine rulings evolve within revelation.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid classifies the types of abrogation. The author provides concrete examples from the Quran and Sunnah, illustrating how later rulings can modify earlier ones across both sources. Understanding these categories is essential for grasping how Islamic law accommodates new circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall classification.
The passage is a single sentence listing four types.
2
Identify the four types
List the four types: abrogation of the Quran by the Quran, the Sunnah by the Sunnah, the Sunnah by the Quran, and the Quran by the Sunnah.
3
Examine the examples
For each type, note the specific example given and understand how it illustrates the abrogation relationship.
The example for Quran by Quran involves the verses on widows (2:240 abrogated by 2:234).
4
Reflect on the disagreement
Notice that the fourth type (Quran by Sunnah) is controversial. The passage affirms it as correct, contrary to those who reject it.
The example is the bequest for parents and near relatives (2:180) abrogated by the Sunnah on inheritance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشمل البعض في النظم ناسخا كان أو منسوخا: نسخ الكتاب بالكتاب كحكم ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنكُمْ وَيَذَرُونَ أَزْوَاجًا وَصِيَّةً لأَزواجهم﴾ بحكم ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنكُمْ وَيَذَرُونَ أَزْوَاجًا يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنفُسِهِنَّ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ وَعَشْرًا﴾ لتأخرها نزولا وإن تقدمت تلاوة، ونسخ السنة بالسنة كحديث 'كنت نهيتكم عن زيارة القبور فزوروها'، والسنة بالكتاب كحكم استقبال بيت المقدس الثابت بالسنة الفعلية باستقبال الكعبة الثابت بقوله تعالى ﴿فَوَلِّ وَجْهَكَ شَطْرَ الْمَسْجِدِ الْحَرَامِ﴾، والكتاب بالسنة ولو آحادا على الصحيح خلافا لمن منعه كجواز الوصية للوالدين والأقربين الدال عليه قوله تعالى ﴿كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمْ﴾.
And it included the part [i.e., abrogation] in the [poetic] text, whether abrogating or abrogated: abrogation of the Book by the Book, like the ruling of 'And those who are taken in death among you and leave wives, a bequest for their spouses' [2:240] abrogated by the ruling 'And those who are taken in death among you and leave wives, they shall wait for four months and ten days' [2:234], because it was revealed later even if it comes earlier in recitation; and abrogation of the Sunnah by the Sunnah, like the hadith 'I had forbidden you from visiting graves, so visit them'; and the Sunnah by the Book, like the ruling of facing Jerusalem established by the actual Sunnah [being abrogated] by facing the Kaaba established by His saying, the Exalted, 'So turn your face toward al-Masjid al-Haram' [2:144]; and the Book by the Sunnah, even if ahad, according to the correct view, contrary to those who prohibit it, like the permissibility of bequest for parents and near relatives indicated by His saying, the Exalted, 'Prescribed for you' [2:180].
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four types of naskh (abrogation) with examples, clarifying that the 'part' (al-ba'd) mentioned in the poem includes both the abrogating and the abrogated. The first type: abrogation of the Quran by the Quran, exemplified by the abrogation of the bequest for widows (2:240) by the waiting period verse (2:234), which was revealed later although it appears earlier in the Quranic text. The second type: abrogation of the Sunnah by the Sunnah, as in the hadith where the Prophet lifted the previous prohibition on visiting graves. The third type: abrogation of the Sunnah by the Quran, as the practice of facing Jerusalem (established by the Prophet's actual action) was abrogated by the Quranic command to face the Kaaba (2:144). The fourth type: abrogation of the Quran by the Sunnah, even by ahad (solitary) reports, according to the correct (sahih) view, contrary to those who reject this; the example is the permissibility of bequest for parents and near relatives (2:180), which was later abrogated by the Sunnah of the Prophet regarding inheritance shares.

Key Terms
نسخ الكتاب بالكتاب
نسخ السنة بالسنة
نسخ السنة بالكتاب
نسخ الكتاب بالسنة
آحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That abrogation can only occur from Quran to Quran, or that Sunnah cannot abrogate Quran.
Correction: The passage shows that abrogation of the Quran by the Sunnah is valid according to the correct view, with the example of the bequest verse being abrogated by the Sunnah on inheritance.

Checkpoint

B) Sunnah abrogating Sunnah
C) Sunnah abrogated by Quran
D) Quran abrogated by Sunnah

Key Takeaway
Islamic law recognizes four types of abrogation, including the abrogation of the Quran by the Sunnah, which is accepted by the majority of scholars.
