The Abundance of the Prophet's Miracles and the Greatness of the Quran
Commentator introduces the abundance of Prophet's miracles, especially the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Prophet Muhammad's miracles are numerous, with the Quran being the greatest and most enduring miracle.

Core Idea
The Prophet's miracles are countless; the Quran is the greatest.

This lesson examines the section on prophethood from Jawharat al-Tawhid. The commentator highlights the immense number of miracles granted to Prophet Muhammad (saw), emphasizing that the Quran is the greatest among them. We will explore the definition of a miracle and why the Quran holds a special status.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage from the matn and its commentary carefully. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Pay attention to the line of poetry that summarizes the abundance of miracles.
2
Identify the key claim about miracles
The passage states that the Prophet's miracles are many and brilliant. Identify the phrase that emphasizes this.
The commentator explicitly says 'his miracles are many, brilliant'.
3
Understand the definition of a miracle
The commentary defines a miracle as an extraordinary occurrence that appears at the hand of a prophet, proving his truthfulness. Note that it can be with or without a challenge.
The footnote clarifies the scope of the term 'miracle'.
4
Recognize the Quran as the greatest miracle
The passage singles out the Quran as the greatest miracle among the many. Reflect on why the Quran is considered the most significant.
The Quran is the speech of Allah and a miracle for mankind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات
ولمَّا أنهى نصف المنظومةِ وقَدَّم الكلامَ على وجوب الإيمان بمعجزات الأنبياء عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام نَبَّه هنا على كثرتها لنبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم دون غيره بقوله أوَّل النَّصفِ الثاني:
بيانُ مَزيد تشريفه الله بكثرة مُعجزاتِه وأنَّ أعظمَها القُرآن
ومعجزاته كثيرةٌ غُرَرْ منها كلامُ اللهِ مُعْجِزُ البَشَرُ
(وَمُعْجِزَاتُهُ (٤))، أي : خوارقُ العَادةِ الظَّاهرة على يده، الدالة على صدقه.
(4) أراد مُطلق الأمر الخارق، سواء كان عند التحدي أو لا، فهو من استعمال اللفظ في حقيقته ومجازه، أو هو من عموم المجاز. والإضافة في المعجزات لتعظيم المضاف وتشريفه.
Section on Prophethoods. When he finished the first half of the poem and presented the discussion on the obligation of believing in the miracles of the prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, he drew attention here to their abundance for our Prophet Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, to the exclusion of others, by his saying at the beginning of the second half: 'Statement of the increase in Allah's honoring him with the abundance of his miracles and that the greatest of them is the Quran.' 'And his miracles are many, brilliant; among them is the speech of Allah, a miracle for mankind.' (And his miracles (4)), i.e., extraordinary occurrences that appear at his hand, indicating his truthfulness. (4) He intended absolute extraordinary matter, whether it is in the context of a challenge or not, so it is from using the word in its literal and metaphorical sense, or from the generality of metaphor. The construct (idafa) in 'miracles' is for magnifying the attached and honoring it.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage introduces the section on prophethoods, specifically focusing on the miracles of Prophet Muhammad (saw). The commentator states that after completing half of the poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid), the author alerted to the abundance of miracles granted to our Prophet over others. He quotes the opening of the second half: a statement that Allah honored him with numerous miracles, the greatest being the Quran. The commentary then defines 'his miracles' as supernatural events (khawariq al-'ada) that appear at his hand and prove his truthfulness. The footnote clarifies that 'miracle' (mu'jizah) here means any extraordinary occurrence, whether accompanied by a challenge or not, and the idafa (construct phrase) serves to magnify and honor the Prophet.

Key Terms
النبوات
المعجزات
خوارق العادة
التحدي
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are only those that come with a challenge (tahaddi) to produce something similar.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a miracle is any extraordinary event that proves prophethood, whether or not it is accompanied by a challenge. The term 'mu'jizah' is used in a general sense.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Quran is the greatest miracle of Prophet Muhammad, and his miracles are numerous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You say you believe the Quran is the greatest miracle, but when was the last time you truly stood in awe of its verses? What would change in your daily life if you treated the Quran as a living miracle rather than just a book to recite?
?
The Prophet's miracles are many, but the Quran remains with us. Are you using it as the proof it is meant to be? In what area of your life are you still seeking signs when the greatest sign is already in your hands?
?
If the Quran is a miracle that challenges all of humanity, what does that mean for you personally? Do you live as though you have been challenged by it? What is one verse that demands a response from you today, and how will you respond?

The Abundance of the Prophet's Miracles and the Miracle of the Quran
The commentator lists many miracles of Prophet Muhammad, emphasizing their abundance and the Quran as the greatest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) was granted an extraordinary number of miracles, how this abundance sets him apart from other prophets, and why the Quran is considered the greatest among them.

Core Idea
The Prophet's many miracles prove Allah's special favor and his prophethood.

After establishing the obligation to believe in the miracles of all prophets, the poem now turns to the unique abundance of miracles granted to Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). The commentator explains that despite his shorter lifespan, his miracles outnumber those of any other prophet, pointing to Allah's special care. The passage lists several specific miracles, emphasizing the Quran as the greatest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the commentator first gives the context, then the line of poetry, then the explanation with examples.
Pay attention to the list of miracles; they are not exhaustive but indicative.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that the Prophet's miracles are many, more than any other prophet, and that the greatest miracle is the Quran.
Compare with the phrase 'كثرة ما وصل إليها معجزات أحد غيره'.
3
Recognize the list of miracles
The passage lists several miracles (chest splitting, moon splitting, speaking of stones, etc.). Understand that this is not an exhaustive list but an indication of abundance.
Some of these narrations may have different levels of authenticity.
4
Understand the purpose
The commentator notes the purpose is only to point out the abundance, not to discuss the obligation of belief, which was already covered.
The phrase 'والغرض الآن إنما هو التنبيه على كثرة معجزاته' clarifies this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمَّا أنهى نصف المنظومةِ وقَدَّم الكلامَ على وجوب الإيمان بمعجزات الأنبياء عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام نَبَّه هنا على كثرتها لنبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم دون غيره بقوله أوَّل النَّصفِ الثاني:

بيانُ مَزيد تشريفه الله بكثرة مُعجزاتِه وأنَّ أعظمَها القُرآن

(وَمُعْجِزَاتُهُ (٤))، أي : خوارقُ العَادةِ الظَّاهرة على يده ، الدالة على صدق نُبُوَّته كَثِيرَةٌ كَثرة ما وصل إليها مُعجزات أحدٍ غيره من طول مُدَّتِهم وقصر مُدَّتِه ، وذلك أدلُّ دَليل على مزيدِ عناية الله به ، وهو دليل حظ مزيد التشريف(۱)، كشق صدره الشريف، وإخراج العلقة التي هي الشَّيطان من قلبه ، وإخباره عن المُغيَّبات ، كبيت المقدس وما فيه حين تردُّدِهم (۲) في معراجه وسؤالهم له أن يصفه وكانشقاق القمر (۳)، وتسليم الحجر والشجر عليه، وتكليم الظَّبية (٤) ، وتسبيح الحَصى في اتِّخاذ المنبر، وَرَدِّ قتادة الجذع الذي كان يخطبُ إليه قبل ، عين كفه ، وحَنِينِ حين سالت على خده (٥) ، فكانت أحسنَ عَيْنَيْه وأَحدَّهُما نَظرًا، وشهادةِ الضَّب بنبوته،

والغرضُ الآنَ إنما هو التنبيه على كثرة معجزاته، وما تقدم إنما هو في وجوب الإيمان بمعجزات الأنبياء. الشنواني.
And when he finished the first half of the poem and presented the discussion on the obligation of belief in the miracles of the prophets, peace be upon them, he pointed here to their abundance for our Prophet Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, to the exclusion of others, by his saying at the beginning of the second half: Elucidation of the extra honor of God for him by the abundance of his miracles and that the greatest of them is the Qur’an. And his miracles are numerous, including the speech of God, a miracle for mankind. (And his miracles (4)), i.e., extraordinary things (khawāriq al-‘āda) that appear at his hand, indicating the truth of his prophethood, are many, to the extent that the miracles of no other prophet reached that number, despite the length of their lifetimes and the brevity of his. That is the clearest evidence of God’s extra care for him, and it is a proof of the abundance of extra honor (1), such as the splitting of his noble chest, the removal of the blood clot that is the devil from his heart, his informing about unseen things such as the description of Bayt al-Maqdis and what was in it when they hesitated (2) about his Night Journey and asked him to describe it, and the splitting of the moon (3), the greeting of stones and trees to him, the speaking of the deer (4), the glorification of pebbles in his hand when he took the pulpit, the return of Qatada’s date-palm trunk which he used to lean on before his hand, and its [the trunk’s] moaning when it was split and its tears flowed on his cheek (5), and it was the most beautiful of his eyes and the sharpest in sight, and the testimony of a lizard to his prophethood. And the purpose now is only to point out the abundance of his miracles, and what preceded is only on the obligation of belief in the miracles of the prophets. Al-Shanawanī.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage introduces the second half of the poem, which focuses on the miracles of Prophet Muhammad. The commentator states that after completing the first half on the obligation to believe in the miracles of all prophets, the poem now highlights the abundance of miracles specific to Muhammad. The line says: 'His miracles are numerous, including the speech of God (the Quran) which is a miracle for mankind.' Then the commentator explains that 'his miracles' means extraordinary events (khawariq al-‘ada) that appear at his hands and prove his prophethood. They are so numerous that no other prophet had as many, despite their longer lifespans and his shorter one, indicating God's special favor. The commentator then lists several miracles: the splitting of his chest, the removal of the devil from his heart, informing about unseen things like the description of Jerusalem during the Night Journey, the splitting of the moon, the greeting of stones and trees, the speaking of a deer, the glorification of pebbles in his hand, the return of the palm trunk that used to lean on him and its moaning, the miraculous healing of Qatada's eye, and the testimony of a lizard to his prophethood. Finally, the commentator notes that the purpose is only to point out the abundance, as the obligation to believe in the miracles of prophets was already covered.

The Abundance of the Prophet's Miracles as Divine Favor
Al-Laqqani argues that the abundance of the Prophet's miracles proves God's special favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the abundance of the Prophet Muhammad's miracles, despite his short lifetime, is presented as the strongest evidence of God's special favor for him.

Core Idea
The Prophet's many miracles prove God's special care beyond all others.

Al-Laqqani now turns from the general obligation of believing in prophets' miracles to the specific honor of the Prophet Muhammad. He argues that the sheer number of miracles granted to him, despite his shorter prophetic mission, surpasses that of all previous prophets. In this passage, he lists several miracles as examples and concludes that they are too many to count, emphasizing the absolute favor bestowed upon the final messenger.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the flow from the exposition of honor to the comparative argument and then the list of miracles.
The passage is dense with examples; focus on the overall argument first.
2
Analyze the comparative argument
Identify how Al-Laqqani compares the Prophet Muhammad to previous prophets. The key point: despite the shorter duration of his prophethood, he was given more miracles. Why is this significant?
This comparison is the core proof of extra divine care.
3
Reflect on the listed miracles
Go through the list of miracles: chest splitting, description of Bayt al-Maqdis, splitting of the moon, greeting of stones and trees, etc. How do these serve as evidence? The passage states they are examples, not an exhaustive list.
The list underscores the theme of abundance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان مزيد تشريفه بكثرة معجزاته وأعظمها القرآن. الأنبياء مع صدق نبوته كثيرة كثرة ما وصل إليها معجزات أحد غيره من طول مدتهم وقصر مدته، وذلك أدل دليل على مزيد عناية الله به، وهو دليل مزيد التشريف، كشق صدره الشريف، وإخراج العلقة التي هي الشيطان من قلبه، وإخباره عن المغيّبات، كبيت المقدس وما فيه حين ترددهم في معراجه وسؤالهم له أن يصفه، وانشقاق القمر، وتسليم الحجر والشجر عليه، وتكليم الظبية، وتسبيح الحصى في اتخاذ المنبر، ورد قتادة عين كفه، وحنين الجذع الذي كان يخطب إليه قبل، سالت حين على خده، فكانت أحسن عينيه وأحدهما نظرًا، وشهادة الضب بنبوته، والغرض الآن إنما هو التنبيه على كثرة معجزاته. وما تقدم إنما هو في وجوب الإيمان بمعجزات الأنبياء. وغير ذلك مما لا يحصى؛ ولذا وصفها بالكثرة المطلقة عن التقييد بعدد معين أو مبهم إيماء للعجز عن الإحاطة بها.
An exposition of his extra honor by the abundance of his miracles, and the greatest of them is the Quran. The prophets together with the truthfulness of his prophethood are many—the abundance of what has reached of the miracles of no one else besides him, despite the length of their durations and the shortness of his duration. That is the most indicative evidence of the extra care of Allah for him, and it is evidence of extra honor. Such as the splitting of his noble chest, and the removal of the clot which was the devil from his heart, and his informing of unseen things—such as the Holy House (Bayt al-Maqdis) and what was in it when they hesitated regarding his Mi'raj and asked him to describe it—and the splitting of the moon, and the greeting of stones and trees to him, and the speaking of the gazelle, and the glorification of pebbles upon the erection of the pulpit, and the restoration of Qatada's eye to his palm, and the yearning of the stump of the palm tree which he used to deliver sermons upon before, when it flowed upon his cheek, and it became the better of his two eyes and the sharper in sight, and the testimony of the lizard to his prophethood. The aim now is only to alert to the abundance of his miracles. What has preceded is only regarding the obligation of belief in the miracles of the prophets. And other things beyond count; therefore he described them as absolute abundance unrestricted by a specific or vague number, indicating incapacity to encompass them.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a combined enumeration and proof. It begins by stating the topic: an exposition of the Prophet Muhammad's extra honor through the abundance of his miracles, with the Quran as the greatest. Al-Laqqani then makes a striking comparative point: the miracles of all previous prophets combined are numerous, yet the Prophet Muhammad, despite his shorter lifespan, was given even more miracles. This is presented as the clearest evidence of God's special care for him. The commentator then lists specific miracles as examples: the splitting of his chest and removal of the devilish clot from his heart (a reference to the incident of the chest-opening), his informing of unseen things like the description of Bayt al-Maqdis during the Isra' and Mi'raj, the splitting of the moon (a well-known miracle), the greeting of stones and trees, the speaking of a gazelle, the glorification of pebbles when the pulpit was built, the restoration of Qatada's eye after it was injured, the yearning of the palm stump (which used to be his pulpit), and the testimony of a lizard to his prophethood (though the commentator notes elsewhere that some of these are subject to authenticity scrutiny, but here they are presented as part of the list). After the list, he clarifies that the purpose is to highlight the sheer abundance, not to repeat that belief in miracles is obligatory (since that was already covered). He concludes with a remark on the phrase 'al-kathrah' (abundance) in the poem: it is absolute, not tied to a specific count, indicating that the miracles are so many that they cannot be fully enumerated, hence the description of incapacity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the list of miracles is exhaustive or that each must be historically verified individually for the argument to hold.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the purpose is to point to abundance, not to list every miracle; the argument stands on the sheer quantity compared to other prophets, not on the verification of each item.

Key Takeaway
The abundance of the Prophet's miracles, despite his shorter prophetic span, is the clearest sign of Allah's special favor for him.

?
The Prophet ﷺ received extraordinary favors from Allah that no other prophet received in such abundance. What does that tell you about your own relationship with him? If not, what concrete step will you take today to increase that love—perhaps reading more about his miracles or sending salawat with presence?
?
If the abundance of miracles is the clearest evidence of Allah's special care for the Prophet, then what evidence do you have in your own life of Allah's special care for you? Can you identify even one sign of divine care that you have overlooked? Write it down and thank Allah for it right now, or your heart may remain ungrateful.

The Miracle of Isra and Mi'raj: Necessity and Nature
Explanation of the Mi'raj verse, asserting its physical reality and necessary belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation to believe in the Prophet's Mi'raj as a physical, waking journey with body and soul, and the different rulings for denying the Isra versus the Mi'raj.

Core Idea
The Mi'raj was a physical, waking journey; denying the Isra is disbelief, while denying only the Mi'raj is a sin.

The Isra and Mi'raj are among the greatest miracles of the Prophet ﷺ. This lesson establishes the correct Islamic belief regarding this event, clarifying that it was a bodily and spiritual journey in a state of wakefulness, and distinguishes between the rulings for denying the Isra (which is kufr) and denying the Mi'raj (which is disobedience).

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the poem line and commentary.
2
Identify the key claims
List the claims: obligation to believe, the journey was with body and soul, in wakefulness.
3
Examine the evidence
The passage mentions the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus of the second generation as proofs.
This shows the high level of certainty.
4
Understand the ruling
Note the distinction: denying the Isra (origin) is kufr, denying Mi'raj is only a sin.
This is because the Isra is explicitly mentioned in the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقظةً
معجزة الإسراء والمعراج
[ مُعجِزةُ الإِسْرَاءِ والمِعْرَاج]
٧٤ - واجزم بمعراج النَّبِيِّ كما رَوَوْا وبرِّئَنْ لِعَائِشَهُ ممَّا رَمَوْا
(وَاجْزِمْ) اعتقادكَ وجوبًا ( بِمِعْرَاج النَّبي)، أي: بأنَّ مِن جملة مُعجزاتِه وقُوعُ عُروجه وصحة صعوده (١) صلى الله عليه وسلم ، بلا براق صعودها ، بلا براق بعد الإسراء به عليه، بجسمه وروحه، من المسجد الحرام إلى المسجد الأقصى، فصعد من صخرة بيت المقدس إلى سِدْرَة المُنتهى (٢) وحيثُ شاء الله ، حال كون العروج الذي جزمتَ به كَمَا رَوَوا)، أي: مُطابقًا ومُماثلًا للوصف الذي رواه أهل الحديث والتفسير والسِّير، ولشهرة إطلاق أحد الاسمين - أعني الإسراء والمعراج - على ما يعمُّ مَدلوليهما .. استغنى الناظم رحمه الله تعالى عن التَّعرُّض لذكر الإسراء ، وإن كان الواجب التعرّض له ؛ لأنَّه قد أُنكِر (٣) ، والحقُّ
قسم النبوات
كما أشرنا (١) إليه في التَّقرير : أنَّه كان يقظةً بالرُّوح والجسد، من المسجد الحرام إلى المسجد الأقصى، بشهادةِ الكِتاب والسُّنَّة (٢) وإجماع القرن الثاني (٣) مِن الأمة ومن بعدهم، ثمَّ إلى السَّماء، ثمَّ إلى الجنَّة ، ثمَّ إلى المُستوى أو العرش (٥) أو طَرف العالم (٦) ، بالأحاديث المشهورة (٤) من بالروح والجسد معا ، ولا يسع أحدٌ من أهل الإسلام إنكاره ، لأنه تكذيب للقرآن ، ففي إنكاره أصله كفر ، وأما إنكار المعراج فمعصية فقط لا كفر . الشنواني .
In wakefulness. The miracle of Isra and Mi'raj. 74 - And be certain (i.e., believe necessarily) about the Prophet's Mi'raj as they narrated, and absolve Aisha from what they accused. (And be certain) your belief necessarily (about the Prophet's Mi'raj), meaning: that among his miracles is the occurrence of his Ascension and the soundness of his ascent (1), peace be upon him, without a Buraq? his ascent, without a Buraq after the Isra with him, with his body and soul, from the Sacred Mosque to the Farthest Mosque, then he ascended from the Rock of Jerusalem to the Sidrat al-Muntaha (2) and wherever Allah willed, the Ascension about which you are certain being as they narrated, i.e., matching and similar to the description narrated by the people of hadith, tafsir, and sirah. And due to the fame of applying one of the two names – I mean Isra and Mi'raj – to that which encompasses their meanings, the author (may Allah have mercy on him) dispensed with mentioning the Isra, although it was obligatory to mention it, because it had been denied (3). The truth is as we indicated (1) in the commentary: that it was in wakefulness with the spirit and body, from the Sacred Mosque to the Farthest Mosque, by the testimony of the Book and the Sunnah (2) and the consensus of the second century (3) of the community and those after them, then to the heaven, then to Paradise, then to the al-Mustawa or the Throne (5) or the edge of the world (6), by the well-known hadiths (4). With the spirit and body together, and no one from the people of Islam can deny it, because it is a denial of the Quran; thus denying its origin is disbelief, but denying the Mi'raj is only disobedience, not disbelief. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the verse on the Mi'raj, defining its reality and proving its necessity. The commentator begins by expounding the line 'And be certain about the Prophet's Mi'raj as they narrated', stating that it means one must necessarily believe that among the Prophet's miracles is the occurrence of his Ascension (Mi'raj) in a sound manner. He clarifies that this Ascension happened after the Isra (Night Journey), with the Prophet's body and soul, from the Sacred Mosque in Mecca to the Farthest Mosque in Jerusalem, then ascending from the Rock of Jerusalem to the Sidrat al-Muntaha (the Lote Tree of the Utmost Boundary) and beyond as Allah willed. The phrase 'as they narrated' indicates that this belief must match the description transmitted by scholars of hadith, tafsir, and prophetic biography. The commentator notes that the poem's author omitted explicit mention of the Isra because both names (Isra and Mi'raj) are often used interchangeably to cover both events, though strictly speaking it was obligatory to mention the Isra since it had been denied by some. The commentator then reasserts the correct position: that the journey occurred in a waking state, with spirit and body, from the Sacred Mosque to the Farthest Mosque, as testified by the Quran and Sunnah, and by the consensus of the second generation of Muslims (the tabi'un) and those after them. The journey continued to the heavens, Paradise, and then to al-Mustawa or the Throne or the edge of the world, according to well-known hadiths. The commentator concludes with the ruling: denying the Isra (the original journey) constitutes disbelief because it is a denial of the Quran, whereas denying only the Mi'raj (the ascension from Jerusalem) is a sin but not disbelief. The footnote by al-Shanwani adds that the journey was with body and soul together, and no Muslim can deny it.

إسراء

سدرة المنتهى
إجماع القرن الثاني

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mi'raj was only a spiritual dream or vision.
Correction: The passage affirms it was a physical journey in wakefulness with body and soul.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the ruling on denying the Isra (Night Journey)?
A) It is a minor sin
B) It is disbelief (kufr)
C) It is permissible
D) It is the same as denying the Mi'raj

Key Takeaway
Belief in the physical, waking Mi'raj is obligatory; denying the Isra is kufr while denying only the Mi'raj is a sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself rationalizing the miracles of the Prophets to fit a materialistic worldview? Ask yourself: is there any part of the Prophet’s ﷺ life that you struggle to accept as literal? What does that say about your iman?
?
The text says denying the Isra is kufr. Have you ever doubted any clear verse of the Quran? What would you say to Allah if He asked you about that doubt?
?
The Mi'raj is a test of acceptance. Will you accept it as the scholars have narrated, or will your intellect reject it? What are the consequences for your faith if you choose to reinterpret it?

The Obligation to Believe in Aisha's Innocence and Its Proof
Al-Laqqani states the obligation and provides a rational syllogism with two proofs of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Laqqani uses rational proof to establish the obligation of believing in the innocence of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her).

Core Idea
A truthful report about a possible event is necessarily true.

This passage from Al-Laqqani’s Jawharat al-Tawhid demonstrates that core beliefs can be supported by rational arguments. He sets up a simple yet powerful syllogism: the event of Aisha’s innocence is possible; the Prophet ﷺ, who is truthful, reported it; therefore it is true and belief in it is obligatory. He then provides two proofs for the possibility of such an event, grounding it in the general principles of physics and logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the syllogism
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Al-Laqqani states the obligation, then gives the condition (possible matter reported by truthful), and then concludes it is true.
2
Understand the two proofs of possibility
The author gives two ways to prove possibility: similarity of bodies and absence of proof of impossibility. Relate these to examples.
3
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this method applies to other reported events. Why is it important to establish possibility before accepting a report?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوبُ اعتقادِ بَراءةِ أُمّ المؤمنينَ الصّديقةِ بنت الصديق. وهو أمر ممكن أخبر به الصَّادقُ ، وكلُّ ما هو كذلك فهو حقٌّ ، وحكمه مطابق ؛ ودليل الإمكان: إمَّا تماثل الأجسام، فيجوز على السماوات الخَرْقُ والالتئام ، كما يجوزان على الأرض والماء ، ويجوز على الإنسان سُرعة قطع المسَافَة كما يجوز على الطير والريح ؛ وإما عدم دليل الامتناع ؛ وهو أنَّه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعِه محال .
The obligation of believing in the innocence of the Mother of the Believers, the Truthful (al-Siddiqah), daughter of al-Siddiq. And it is a possible matter (amr mumkin) reported by the truthful (al-sadiq), and everything that is like that is true (haqq), and its ruling is corresponding (mutabiq); and the proof of possibility (dalil al-imkan): either the similarity of bodies (tamathul al-ajsam), so tearing and rejoining are permissible for the heavens, just as they are permissible for the earth and water, and for a human to quickly traverse a distance is permissible as it is for a bird and the wind; or the absence of a proof of impossibility (dafil al-imtina'), which is that no impossibility (muhal) follows from assuming its occurrence.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the obligation to believe in the innocence of Aisha (the Mother of the Believers). It begins by stating the obligation itself. Then it constructs a syllogism: the event of her innocence is a possible matter (amr mumkin) reported by the truthful (the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him). Whatever is reported by the truthful about a possible matter is necessarily true (haqq), and its ruling corresponds to reality. Therefore, the belief in her innocence is obligatory. The author then provides the proof of possibility (dalil al-imkan): possibility is established either by the similarity of bodies (tamathul al-ajsam)—since all bodies are similar in essence, what is possible for one is possible for another, such as the tearing and rejoining of the heavens being possible just as it is on earth, or a human traveling quickly like a bird; or by the absence of a proof of impossibility, meaning that no logical contradiction arises from supposing its occurrence. Thus, the event is categorically possible, and with the truthful report, belief in it is incumbent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Extraordinary events like Aisha's innocence are impossible because they violate natural laws.
Correction: The proof shows that such events are merely possible (amr mumkin) because they do not entail logical impossibility, and all bodies share similar possibilities.

Checkpoint
What are the two proofs given for the possibility of the event of Aisha's innocence?
A) The similarity of bodies and the absence of a proof of impossibility
B) The truthfulness of the Prophet and the consensus of the community
C) The verses of the Quran and the authentic hadiths
D) The miracle of the Prophet and the virtue of Aisha

Key Takeaway
Belief in the innocence of Aisha is not just an article of faith but also a rationally supported truth based on possibility and truthful testimony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Is there a divine command or prophetic report you find difficult to follow because it seems unreasonable or extraordinary to you? How does today's lesson challenge you to submit your intellect to the truthful report?
?

The Obligation to Believe in Aisha's Innocence
The author narrates the incident of the slander and the revelation of ten verses from Surah al-Nur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why believing in Aisha's innocence is a legal obligation (wajib) and how the Qur'an, consensus, and hadith affirm this.

Core Idea
Aisha's innocence is a divine decree, not hearsay.

The story of the slander (ifk) is not just history; it is a foundational incident that establishes a binding belief. In this passage, the author presents the event and the sources that make it obligatory to believe in Aisha's purity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the emphasis on obligation (yajibu shar'an).
The passage is dense with details—focus on the main claim first.
2
Identify the obligation
The author states it is legally obligatory upon every mukallaf to believe in Aisha's innocence. Underline this phrase.
3
Understand the story
Follow the narrative: the lost necklace, howdah mistake, Safwan's help, and the accusation. Reflect on how these details build the context.
The incident occurs after the expedition of Banu al-Mustaliq.
4
Note the three proofs
The author cites the Qur'an (ten verses), consensus of the ummah, and authentic hadith. These are the sources that make the belief obligatory.
This shows the incident is not left to personal opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وبَر أَنْ) يعني : أنَّه يجب شرعاً على كلِّ مكلَّف أنْ يعتقد براءةَ أمّ المؤمنين (لِعَائِشَة) بنت أبي بكر الصديق (مِمَّا رَمَوْا)، أي: من الإفك الذي رَمَاها به المُنافقونَ وقَدْفُوها به ، وكانَ الذي تولى كبره عبد الله بن أُبي بن سلول لعنه الله ، كما جاءَ به القرآنُ وانعقد عليه إجماع الأمة، ووردَتْ به الأحاديثُ الصَّحيحة ؛ حين كانتْ في غزوة بني المصطلق ، تخلّفتْ في طَلب عِقْدِها ، وكانَ مِن جَزْع أظفار ، فَحُمِل هَوْدَجُها ظنَّا أَنَّها فيه ، وسَارَ القومُ ورجعت فلم تجدهم ، فمرَّ بها صفوان بن المُعطّل ، فحملها ولم ينظر إليها وقاد بها البعير موليها ظهره ، حتّى أدرك بها النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فرموها به ، فأنزل الله تعالى فى براءتها عشر آيات من أول سورة النُّور .
(And it is obligatory to [believe in the innocence of]) meaning: that it is legally obligatory (wajib shar'an) upon every legally responsible person (mukallaf) to believe in the innocence of the Mother of the Believers Aisha, daughter of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, from that which they [the slanderers] threw at her, i.e., from the slander (al-ifk) that the hypocrites threw at her and accused her of, and the one who undertook its greatest part was Abdullah ibn Ubayy ibn Salul, may Allah curse him, as has come in the Qur'an, and the consensus of the community (ijma' al-ummah) has been concluded upon it, and authentic hadiths have been reported concerning it; when she was in the expedition of Banu al-Mustaliq, she stayed behind seeking her necklace, and it was made of Yemeni onyx beads, so her howdah was carried thinking she was inside it, and the people departed, and she returned and did not find them, then Safwan ibn al-Mu'attal passed by her, so he made his camel kneel for her, and he did not look at her, and he led the camel turning his back to her until he reached the Prophet (peace be upon him) with her, so they slandered her, then Allah the Exalted revealed concerning her innocence ten verses from the beginning of Surah al-Nur.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative that serves as the evidence for the obligation to believe in Aisha's innocence. It begins by stating the legal obligation (wajib shar'an) for every mukallaf to believe in her innocence from the slander (al-ifk) made by the hypocrites, led by Abdullah ibn Ubayy. The author then provides the sources of this obligation: the Qur'an, the consensus of the community (ijma'), and authentic hadiths. The narrative recounts the incident during the expedition of Banu al-Mustaliq: Aisha stayed behind to search for her necklace made of Yemeni onyx (jaz' afaar), and she was left behind; Safwan ibn al-Mu'attal found her and brought her to the army. The hypocrites then spread the slander. In response, Allah revealed ten verses from the beginning of Surah al-Nur declaring her innocence. This story confirms that the belief in her innocence is not a matter of personal opinion but is divinely mandated through explicit revelation and corroborated by consensus and hadith.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The slander incident is just a historical story with no doctrinal implications.
Correction: The passage explicitly states it is legally obligatory (wajib shar'an) upon every mukallaf to believe in Aisha's innocence, based on Qur'an, consensus, and authentic hadith.

Key Takeaway
Believing in Aisha's innocence is a required belief, established by the Qur'an, consensus, and authentic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The slander was initiated by hypocrites. In your own interactions, do you ever find yourself participating in gossip or spreading unverified claims about others? How can you use this lesson to guard your tongue and honor the Prophet's family?

Definition of Qarn and the Three Generations
Definition of qarn and enumeration of three generations with time periods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the term 'qarn' (century/generation) and the specific durations of the three virtuous generations.

Core Idea
A qarn is a linked generation of people sharing a common purpose.

The author begins by defining the key term 'qarn' (century/generation) which is essential for understanding the hadith about the best generations. This lesson clarifies what a 'qarn' is and establishes the timeframes for the three generations mentioned in the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of qarn
Read the linguistic definition: a group of people from a close time period who share a common purpose. Note the derivation from 'yaqrun' (to link).
2
Apply to the three generations
See how the term applies to the Companions, Followers, and Followers of the Followers.
3
Note the specific years
Memorize the approximate time spans: Companions 120 years, Followers ~70 years, Followers of the Followers ~50 years.
These are based on the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقَرْن: أهل زمانٍ واحدٍ مُتقارب، اشتركوا في أمرٍ من الأمور المقصودة. وسُمِّي قرناً لأنَّه يَقْرِنُ أُمَّةً بأُمَّةٍ وعالماً بعالم، ثم جُعل اسماً للوقت أو لأهله، فقرن الصحابة مُدَّة أصحابه من البعث إلى آخر من مات منهم، وهي مائة وعشرون سنة، أو نفس أصحابه، وقرن التابعين: مِن سنة مائة إلى نحو سبعين، وقرن أتباع التابعين: تمَّ إلى حدود العشرين ومائتين، والله أعلم. وقوله: (فَاسْتَمِعْ) تكملةٌ. فَتَابِعِيْ يعني أَنَّ رُتبتَهم تَلِي رتبة الصَّحابة مِن غير تراخ كبير. والتّابعيُّ: مَن لَقي الصَّحابي الَّذِي لَقِيه صلى الله عليه وسلم، حيًّا مؤمناً حيَّا مؤمناً به، لقيا على غير وجه حرق العادة. وقيل: لا يكفي مجرَّد اللقاء، بل لا بُدَّ مِن الصحبة لمزيَّة لقائه على لقاء غيره من صلحاء أمَّتِه، ولا يشترط فيه التمييز، ولو شُرِط في الصَّحابي لمزيد شرف الصُّحبة. فَتَابِعُ لِمَنْ تَبِعْ يعني أَنَّ رُتبة تابع التابعين تلي رتبة التابعين في الفضل، والأصل في هذا الترتيب قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «خيرُ أُمَّتِي القَرْنُ الذين يلوني، ثُمَّ الَّذِينَ يَلُونَهم، ثُمَّ الَّذِينَ يَلُونَهم» فيه أنَّ الصَّحابة أفضلُ مِن التّابعين، وأنَّ التَّابعين أفضلُ مِن أتباع التابعين، والجمهور على أنَّ هذه الأفضليَّةَ بالنِّسبَة إلى الأفراد، وظاهره أنَّ ما بعد القرون الثلاثة في الفَضيلة سَواءٌ، لا مزيَّةَ لأحدِها على الأُخرى، وذهبَ جماعةٌ إلى تفاوتِ بقيَّة القُرونِ بالسَّبْقِيَّة، فكلُّ قرنٍ أفضلُ من الذي بعده إلى يوم القيامة؛ لحديث: «ما مِن يوم إلَّا والذي بعده شرٌّ مِنه، وإِنَّما يُسرَعُ بخياركُم»
The century (qarn) is the people of one closely connected time period, who share in some intended matter. It was called a century because it links (yaqrun) a nation with a nation and a world with a world, then it became a name for the time or its people. So the century of the Companions (sahaba) is the duration of his Companions from the mission to the last one who died among them, which is one hundred and twenty years, or the Companions themselves. The century of the Followers (tabi'un) is from year one hundred to about seventy, and the century of the Followers of the Followers extends to around two hundred and twenty, and God knows best. And His saying: 'Listen' is a completion. 'Then their follower' means that their rank follows the rank of the Companions without great delay. The Follower (tabi'i) is one who met the Companion who met him (the Prophet), peace be upon him, alive and believing, alive and believing in him, in a meeting that is not a miracle. It is said: mere meeting is not enough, but rather companionship (suhba) is necessary due to the virtue of meeting him over meeting other righteous people of his nation, and discernment (tamyiz) is not required in him, even if it is required in the Companion for the added honor of companionship. 'Then the follower of the one who followed' means that the rank of the Follower of the Followers follows the rank of the Followers in virtue. The basis for this order is his saying, peace be upon him: 'The best of my nation are the century that I am sent to, then those who follow them, then those who follow them.' In it is that the Companions are better than the Followers, and the Followers are better than the Followers of the Followers. The majority hold that this virtue applies to individuals, and its apparent meaning is that after the three centuries there is equality in virtue, with no superiority of one over the other. And a group held that the remaining centuries vary in virtue by precedence, so each century is better than the one after it until the Day of Resurrection, due to the hadith: 'There is no day except that the one after it is worse than it, and indeed the best among you will be taken quickly.'
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage provides definitions and a discussion of the virtuous generations. It begins by defining the term 'qarn' (century/generation) as a group of people from a closely connected period who share a common purpose. The name is derived from the idea of linking one community to another. The author specifies three key generations: the Companions (sahaba), whose period lasts 120 years from the Prophet's mission; the Followers (tabi'un), from about year 100 AH to 170 AH; and the Followers of the Followers (tabi'u al-tabi'in), extending to around 220 AH. The definition of a 'tabi'i' (Follower) is then given: one who met a Companion who had met the Prophet while the Companion was alive and a believer, in a normal meeting. There is a disagreement: some require more than mere meeting, insisting on companionship (suhba) to match the virtue of meeting the Prophet, but discernment (tamyiz) is not required for the Follower. The passage then explains the order of virtue based on the hadith: the Companions are best, then the Followers, then the Followers of the Followers. The majority hold this applies to individuals, and after these three, all later generations are equal in virtue. However, a minority opinion holds that each subsequent century is inferior due to a hadith about increasing evil. The author presents these views without choosing between them.

Key Terms
قرن
صحابة
تابعون
أتباع التابعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the duration of the century of the Companions (sahaba)?
A) From the mission to the end of the caliphate
B) One hundred and twenty years
C) From year one hundred to one hundred seventy
D) Until the death of the last Companion

Key Takeaway
The three virtuous generations are defined by specific time periods: Companions (120 years), Followers (70 years), and Followers of the Followers (50 years).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live in a time far removed from these three generations. Do you feel a real connection to the sahaba, tabi'un, and tabi'u al-tabi'in? How does that connection—or lack of it—affect your relationship with the Prophet and your commitment to the deen? What one step can you take this week to strengthen that link?
?
The author defines a qarn as a group sharing a common purpose. What is the common purpose that defines your generation of Muslims today? Are you actively part of that purpose, or have you drifted into individualism and worldly aims? What will you change to align with that purpose starting today?
?
Imagine yourself as a Companion living in the first 120 years. Would your faith, sacrifice, and loyalty match theirs? If not, what is holding you back right now from embodying that same spirit? Commit to one concrete action this week that brings you closer to their example.

Definition of Tabi'i and Virtue of the Generations
Definition of tabi'i and discussion of virtue order including two opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a Follower (tabi'i) and the differing opinions on the virtue of generations after the first three.

Core Idea
The Follower is one who met a Companion while the Companion had met the Prophet.

After defining the generations, the author explains who qualifies as a Follower (tabi'i) and discusses the conditions for this status. He then addresses the order of virtue among these generations and presents the majority view versus a minority opinion on later generations.

Guided Path
1
Learn the definition of tabi'i
Read the condition: meeting a Companion who met the Prophet, alive and believing, in a normal meeting.
2
Understand the disagreement
Note that some require companionship (suhba) beyond mere meeting.
3
Grasp the order of virtue
From the hadith: Companions best, then Followers, then Followers of the Followers.
4
Compare the two opinions
Majority: equality after the three; minority: each century worse than the previous.

It begins by defining the term 'qarn' (century/generation) as a group of people from a closely connected period who share a common purpose. The name is derived from the idea of linking one community to another. The author specifies three key generations: the Companions (sahaba), whose period lasts 120 years from the Prophet's mission; the Followers (tabi'un), from about year 100 AH to 170 AH; and the Followers of the Followers (tabi'u al-tabi'in), extending to around 220 AH. The definition of a 'tabi'i' (Follower) is then given: one who met a Companion who had met the Prophet while the Companion was alive and a believer, in a normal meeting. There is a disagreement: some require more than mere meeting, insisting on companionship (suhba) to match the virtue of meeting the Prophet, but discernment (tamyiz) is not required for the Follower. The passage then explains the order of virtue based on the hadith: the Companions are best, then the Followers, then the Followers of the Followers. The majority hold this applies to individuals, and after these three, all later generations are equal in virtue. However, a minority opinion holds that each subsequent century is inferior due to a hadith about increasing evil. The author presents these views without choosing between them.

Key Terms
تابعي
صحابي
صحبة
تمييز
أفضلية
قرن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The virtue of the three generations applies to the group collectively, so every Companion is better than every Follower.
Correction: The majority hold that the virtue applies to individuals, so a righteous person in a later generation could be better than an ordinary person in an earlier generation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary condition for being a Follower (tabi'i)?
A) Meeting the Prophet directly
B) Meeting a Companion who met the Prophet while both were alive and believing
C) Living during the first century
D) Being a Muslim and having vision

Key Takeaway
A Follower is defined by meeting a Companion, and the three generations are ranked in virtue by the hadith, but later generations are either equal or decreasing in virtue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the Followers are defined by meeting a Companion. When you meet a scholar or a pious person, do you value that meeting as a spiritual opportunity? How can you ensure you don't take such meetings for granted?
?
The hadith says the best generations are the early ones. If later generations are less virtuous, what does that mean for your own accountability? Are you striving to be among the best of your generation, or are you content with mediocrity?
?
Some scholars hold that each generation after the first three is inferior. If that is true, what urgent steps are you taking to preserve and increase your faith in a time of decline? Write one concrete change you will make this week to strengthen your connection to the Prophet's teachings.

The Best Companions Are the Four Caliphs in Order
The author states that the best Companions are the four caliphs in order of succession and defines the caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the best of the Companions are the four caliphs in the order of their succession, and that this is a matter of obligatory belief.

Core Idea
The order of virtue among the caliphs mirrors the order of their caliphate.

This lesson addresses a fundamental point of Sunni creed: who among the Companions is best? The author clarifies that the four rightly guided caliphs hold the highest rank, and their virtue follows the sequence of their rule. Understanding this helps us affirm the proper respect for the early leaders of Islam as a matter of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the language, then read the English translation. Notice how the matn line is followed by a commentary that expands its meaning.
The line number ٧٦ is part of the matn.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage asserts that the best Companions are the four caliphs in the order of their caliphate. This is presented as obligatory belief.
The phrase 'their matter in virtue is like the caliphate' connects virtue directly to succession order.
3
Understand the definition of caliphate
The commentary defines the supreme caliphate as deputyship on behalf of God for the general welfare of Muslims, including establishing religion and protecting the community. It also mentions the prophetic hadith limiting its duration to thirty years.
This definition shows the caliphate's sacred purpose.
4
Reflect on the obligatory nature
The author explicitly says this is an obligatory belief (hukm wajib al-i'tiqad). Consider why this is important for a Muslim's creed.
Holding this belief is part of correct faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانُ أنَّ أفضل الصحابةِ الخُلَفاء الأربعةُ على ترتيب الخِلَافة
٧٦ - وخيرهم من وُليَ الخِلافَة وَأَمْرُهُم فِي الفَضْل كالخِلافَة

Explanation that the best of the Companions are the four caliphs in the order of the caliphate.
76 - And the best of them are those who undertook the caliphate, and their matter in virtue is like the caliphate.
And he indicated an obligatory belief also by his saying: 'And the best of them' meaning the best of its (the Companions') members absolutely: 'he who was entrusted with [the caliphate]' meaning the persons who were entrusted with the supreme caliphate; which is deputyship on behalf of God in the general interests of the Muslims, in establishing the religion and protecting the Muslims, whose duration is estimated by his saying, peace be upon him: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years.'
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage states the explicit belief that the best of the Companions are the four caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) in the order of their succession. The author explains that the matn line 'And the best of them are those who undertook the caliphate' refers to the individuals who held the supreme caliphate, which is defined as deputyship on behalf of God for the general welfare of the Muslims, including establishing religion and protecting the community. The duration of this caliphate is limited to thirty years as per the hadith. The order of their virtue mirrors the order of their caliphate, meaning Abu Bakr is best, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. This is presented as a point of obligatory belief (i'tiqad wajib).

Correction: The passage explicitly states that their virtue is tied to the order of their caliphate, making this a matter of obligatory belief. Personal merit is not the sole determinant.

Angels' Badr Attendance and the Virtue of Uhud
Rules on angelic and human rankings based on battle attendance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attendance of angels at Badr establishes a hierarchy among them, and how the participants of Uhud are ranked relative to the remaining Badr participants.

Core Idea
Angels who witnessed Badr outrank those who did not; Uhud's people come after remaining Badris.

This lesson continues the discussion on prophethood by addressing a potential objection about angelic ranks. It clarifies that among angels, those who were present at Badr hold a higher station, and this logic extends to believing jinn. Then it turns to the battle of Uhud, explaining its participants' rank relative to those of Badr, with important nuances about individuals who attended both.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice that it begins by linking back to the earlier discussion on prophethood.
Pay attention to the footnoted numbers in the Arabic – they refer to extensive commentaries.
2
Identify the two key rulings
First, the superiority of Badr-attending angels over others. Second, the ranking of Uhud participants after the remaining Badris. Note the qualifier 'remaining'.
The qualifier 'remaining' is crucial for understanding who is excluded.
3
Consider the implications
Ask how this ranking applies to those who attended both battles. The footnote clarifies that Badri virtue is not lost by later attendance at Uhud.
This ties back to the learning objective.

(فَأَهْلُ) غزوة (أُخذ )(۳) - جبل معروف بالمدينة (٤) ـ، رتبتهم تلي — رتبة بقيَّة أهل بدر (ه) ، والمراد : مَن شَهدها من المسلمينَ ، سواءٌ اسْتُشْهِدَ بها كالسَّبعين أم لا ، وكان أَهلُها أَلْفًا بثلاثمئة من المنافقين الذين رجعَ بهم أهل

(۳) قوله: (أحد) بدرج الهمزة وسكون دال أحد الأمير . (٤) قوله: (جبل معروف .. قال فيه : أحدٌ جبل يحبنا ونحبه) أي: يحبنا أهله، وهم الأنصار ، فنسبت المحبة إليه على سبيل المجاز ، أو يُقال: لا مانع أن الله تعالى أودع فيه إدراكا حتى صار يحب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، قيل : فيه قبر هارون أخي موسى ، والأصح أنه في جبل من جبال التيه. وسُمي بأحدٍ: لتوحده عن الجبال . الشنواني . (٥) قوله: (تلي رتبة بقية أهل بدر) والبقية هم الذين لم يحضروا أحدا من البدريين، أما من حضر بدرا ثم أحدا ، فلا يقال مرتبته بعد أهل بدر ، إذ حضوره بدرا لا يسلح عنه ما ثبت له من فضيلة ، وهكذا يُقال في الباقي . الشنواني.
Section on Prophethood: He refutes it by what has preceded: that the rank of angels follows the rank of prophets. Among the angels, those who witnessed Badr are better than those who did not witness it, because of superiority. Yes, and by analogy it may be said likewise concerning the believing jinn. And he restricts by the description of Badr, which is 'the great status', to distinguish it from its other two battles, for its battles are three (2), the greatest of them is the middle one, due to the presence of angels and jinn with humankind. (So the people of) the battle of Uhud (3) – a mountain known in Medina (4) – their rank follows the rank of the remaining people of Badr (5). And what is meant is whoever witnessed it from the Muslims, whether he was martyred there like the seventy or not. And its people were one thousand three hundred, including the hypocrites who returned with them. (1) His saying: 'And what the apparent [meaning] of the text suggests...' The point of suggestion is that the text ruled that the six are better than the angels, so it implies a delay in the virtue of the angels of Badr relative to the six, despite the angels being better. Al-Shanwani. (2) His saying: 'For its battles are three': The first did not have fighting, but was in pursuit of a man called Karz ibn Jabir al-Fihri, who raided the cattle of Medina, and they went out in search of him but did not find him. The third involved the appointment between the Prophet and Abu Sufyan, but Abu Sufyan stayed away for fear when the time came. As for the second, it is the major one, and the first matter in which honor came to Islam. Al-Shanwani. (3) His saying: 'Uhud' – with kasra of the hamza and sukun of the dal, 'Uḥud' – al-Amir. (4) His saying: 'a known mountain...' He said about it: 'Uhud is a mountain that loves us and we love it,' i.e. its people love us, namely the Ansar, so love is attributed to it figuratively, or it is said: there is no preventing that Allah instilled in it perception so that it came to love the Messenger of Allah. It is said: in it is the grave of Harun, brother of Musa, but the more correct [view] is that it is in a mountain of the Tih. And it was named Uhud because of its isolation from the mountains. Al-Shanwani. (5) His saying: 'follows the rank of the remaining people of Badr' – the 'remaining' are those from the Badris who did not attend Uhud. As for one who attended Badr and then Uhud, it is not said that his rank is after the people of Badr, for his presence at Badr does not remove the virtue established for him. And thus it is said concerning the rest. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage begins with 'Section on Prophethood' and refutes an objection. It argues that among angels, those who witnessed Badr are superior to those who did not, and similarly for believing jinn. The description 'great status' distinguishes the famous Badr from two other lesser battles of the same name. The middle Badr is the greatest. Then it turns to Uhud: its people are ranked after the remaining Badris (i.e., those Badris who did not attend Uhud). The author clarifies that a Badri who also attended Uhud retains his Badri virtue. The footnotes provide: 1) explanation of why the text might suggest the six are better than angels; 2) details of the three Badr battles; 3) correct pronunciation of 'Uhud'; 4) description of Mount Uhud and its love for the Prophet; 5) clarification of 'remaining' Badris.

Ranking the Prophet's Household and Maryam: A Scholarly Debate
Commentary on the ranking of the Prophet's wives, daughters, and Maryam with scholarly opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly rankings of the Prophet's wives, daughters, and Maryam, and the evidence used in these debates.

Core Idea
The ranking of holy women reveals deeper theological discussions about prophethood and virtue.

This passage delves into the relative merits of the Prophet's closest family members and other revered women like Maryam. The commentary presents varying scholarly opinions, supported by a hadith, showing that even among the best of women, there is a hierarchy that reflects their unique roles in Islamic history.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the different names and the scholarly disagreement.
2
Identify the rankings
Find the ranking given by Ibn al-Imad, then the one by al-Subki. Note how they differ.
Ibn al-Imad preferred Khadija over Aisha, while al-Subki placed Fatima first.
3
Examine the hadith
Read the hadith cited by al-Subki. It lists four women in order. Understand why this hadith is used to argue Maryam's superiority.
The hadith is: 'The best women of the worlds are Maryam bint Imran, then Khadija, then Fatima, then Asiya.'
4
Reflect on the disagreement
Consider why there is disagreement about prophethood and how that influences the ranking. This shows the depth of theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[تَفاضُل زوجاته وبناته فيما بينهنَّ ومع نساء العالمين] وأما تفضيلُ الزَّوجات الشَّريفات، فأفضلهنَّ: خديجة، وعائشة، وفي أفضلهما خِلافٌ ، صحَّحَ ابنُ العماد (۳) تفضيل خديجة . وفاطمة (١) ، فتكون أفضل من عائشة . ولمَّا سُئل السُّبكي (۲) عن ذلك (۳) قال : الَّذِي نختارهُ ونَدِينُ اللَّهَ بِه أَنَّ فاطمة بنت سيدنا محمد الله ، أفضل ، ثمَّ أَمُّها خديجة ، ثمَّ عائشة . صلى الله عليه وسلم واختار السبكي : أنَّ مريمَ أفضلُ مِن خديجة (٤) ، لقوله : «خير نساء العالَمِينَ مريم بنت عمران ثمَّ خديجة بنت خويلد، ثمَّ فاطمة بنت محمد ، ثم آسية امرأة فرعون » ( ه ) ، وللاختلاف في نبوّتها .
[The preference of his wives and daughters among themselves and with the women of the worlds] As for the preference of the noble wives, the best among them are Khadija and Aisha, and there is disagreement as to which is better. Ibn al-Imad confirmed the preference of Khadija. And Fatima, so she is better than Aisha. When al-Subki was asked about that, he said: What we choose and hold as our religion before God is that Fatima bint our master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is best, then her mother Khadija, then Aisha. And al-Subki chose that Maryam is better than Khadija, because of his saying: 'The best women of the worlds are Maryam bint Imran, then Khadija bint Khuwaylid, then Fatima bint Muhammad, then Asiya the wife of Pharaoh,' and due to the disagreement about her prophethood.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ranking of the Prophet's wives and daughters and compares them to Maryam and Asiya. It begins with a heading from the matn: 'The preference of his wives and daughters among themselves and with the women of the worlds.' The commentary then states that the best of the noble wives are Khadija and Aisha, and there is a disagreement as to which is superior. Ibn al-Imad validated the preference of Khadija. Then it mentions Fatima, noting that she is better than Aisha. It then presents the opinion of al-Subki, who when asked about the ranking said: 'What we choose and hold as our religion before God is that Fatima bint our master Muhammad is best, then her mother Khadija, then Aisha.' Al-Subki also chose that Maryam is better than Khadija, citing a hadith: 'The best women of the worlds are Maryam bint Imran, then Khadija bint Khuwaylid, then Fatima bint Muhammad, then Asiya the wife of Pharaoh.' The commentary adds that this is due to the disagreement about Maryam's prophethood. The lesson covers the internal ranking of the Prophet's immediate family and then extends to include Maryam and Asiya from outside the family, showing a range of scholarly opinions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proofs for the Possibility and Occurrence of Karama
The author presents rational and scriptural proofs for the possibility and occurrence of karama.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and scriptural proofs for the possibility and actual occurrence of karama (saintly wonders) as presented in classical Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Karama is possible because it is inherently possible; it has occurred as proven by scripture and tradition.

This lesson presents two key arguments used by Sunni scholars to defend the reality of karama (miraculous wonders of saints). First, a rational argument demonstrates that such wonders are within God's power and entail no impossibility. Second, a scriptural argument cites Qur'anic examples and continuous reports to confirm their historical occurrence. Understanding these proofs clarifies the nature of saintly miracles and their distinction from prophetic miracles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational proof
Read the rational argument carefully: the wonder is possible in itself, everything possible is within God's power, and no impossibility follows from assuming its occurrence.
2
Examine the scriptural evidence
Look at the Qur'anic examples mentioned (Maryam, Ashab al-Kahf, Asif) and the claim of continuous karamat among the Companions and later generations.
These are not exhaustive; they are representative proofs.
3
Reflect on the nature of sainthood
Note the concluding remark that sainthood (wilaya) is not acquired like prophethood – it is a divine gift.
This reinforces that karama is a gracious act, not a earned status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج أصحابنا على الجواز بأنَّ ظهور الخَارِقِ المذكور أمر ممكن نفسه، وكلُّ ما هو كذلك فهو صالح لشمول القدرة لإيجاده، ودليلُ جواز ذلك الأمر وإمكانه أنه لا يلزم مِن فَرضِ وقوعه مُحالٌ، واحتجوا على الوقوع: بما جاء في الكتاب مِن قِصَّة مريمَ وولادتها عيسى دون زوج، كفالة زكريَّا لها وما وقع لها، وقِصَّةِ أصحاب الكهف؛ ولبثهم سنين بلا طعام ولا شراب، وقصَّة آصف ومجيئه بالعرش قبل ارتداد طرف سليمان إليه، وما وقع من كرامات الصَّحابة والتابعين إلى وقتنا هذا، وليست الولاية مكتسبةً كالنُّبوَّة.
Our companions argued for permissibility by [saying] that the appearance of the mentioned wonder is a matter possible in itself, and everything that is so is fit for the encompassment of power to bring it into existence, and the proof of the permissibility of that matter and its possibility is that no impossibility follows from supposing its occurrence. And they argued for occurrence by what has come in the Book: the story of Mary and her giving birth to Jesus without a husband, Zachariah's sponsorship of her and what occurred for her, and the story of the People of the Cave and their staying for years without food or drink, and the story of Asif and his bringing the throne before the return of Solomon's glance to him, and what has occurred of karamat of the Companions and Followers up to our time. And sainthood is not acquired like prophethood.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs: a rational proof for the possibility of karama, and a scriptural proof for its actual occurrence. For possibility: the wonder is inherently possible; everything possible is within God's power; and no logical impossibility follows from assuming its occurrence. For occurrence: the Qur'an provides examples such as Maryam, Ashab al-Kahf, and Asif, and there are continuous reports of karamat among the Companions and later generations. The passage concludes by noting that wilaya is not acquired like prophethood, emphasizing its divine gift nature.

Key Terms
جواز
وقوع
قدرة
ولاية
مكتسبة
خارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karama is no longer possible after the prophetic era.
Correction: The author affirms that karamat have occurred among the Companions and Followers up to the present time, implying they continue.

Checkpoint
What is the rational proof given for the possibility of karama?
A) It is mentioned in the Qur'an.
B) It is a wonder that is impossible by nature.
C) Its occurrence entails no logical impossibility.
D) It is reported by many trustworthy narrators.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of karama is established by reason (no impossibility), and its occurrence is confirmed by scripture and continuous tradition; sainthood is a divine gift, not an acquired status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If sainthood and its wonders are divine gifts beyond your control, how does that challenge your desire to 'earn' spiritual status through your own deeds? Will you surrender your striving to God's grace, or are you still trying to force open a door that only He opens?
?
The passage says no impossibility follows from karama. What 'impossibilities' in your own life—sins you think you can't overcome, forgiveness you think you don't deserve—are you refusing to let God's power touch because you've deemed them 'impossible'? Do you truly believe that for God, nothing is impossible?
?
The karamat of Mary and Asif were not sought; they were given. How does this free you from the burden of needing to produce extraordinary spiritual results? Can you be content with hidden devotion, trusting that the gift is in His hands?

The Quran as Proof of Prophethood: Why It Is Specified
Explains why the Quran is singled out as proof for prophethood due to tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran is specifically singled out as the proof of prophethood—due to its tawatur (uninterrupted transmission)—and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
The Quran's tawatur makes it the unique proof for prophethood, not its evidential limits.

This passage addresses an implied question: why is the Quran mentioned specifically when discussing proof of prophethood? The author explains that it is because of the Quran's unmatched reliability in transmission (tawatur). He then supports this by noting the Prophet's own use of supplication and the consensus of the community. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author begins with a direct claim about why the Quran is specified.
The footnote marker (1) refers to further commentary on this claim.
2
Identify the key claim and its support
The claim: the Quran is specified due to its tawatur. The support: examples from the Prophet's supplications and consensus of the community. Identify each part.
The author uses 'فقد دعا' (for he supplicated) to provide evidence.
3
Reflect on why tawatur is sufficient
Tawatur ensures certainty. Ask yourself why this makes the Quran a stronger proof than other miracles.
This refutes the Mu'tazila who may have had objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات
سو
وتخصيص القرآنِ لِتواتُره (۱) ، لا لِقَصْرِ الدَّلالة عليه، فقد دعا صلى الله عليه وسلم ربَّهُ في مواطن كثيرة كيوم بدر، وعلى قاتل أهل بئر معونة، وعلى المُستهزئين ، وأجمع عليه السَّلفُ والخَلفُ
ومِن آداب الدُّعاء (۲): تحرّي الأوقات الفاضلة؛ كالسُّجود، وعند الأذان ، ومِنها تقديمُ الوضوء والصَّلاة، واستقبال القبلة، ورفع الأيدي، وتقديمُ التّوبة ، والاعتراف بالذنب والإخلاص، وافتتاحه بالحمد والثناء والصَّلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، والسُّؤال بالأسماء الحُسنى، وختمه بالصَّلاة ، والسَّلام عليه ، وجعلها في وسطه أيضا، والله أعلم.
(۱) جواب عن سؤال مقدر هو : لم خُصَّ ذكر دليل الدعاء من القرآن لا من غيره في النظم ؟ وفيه رد على المعتزلة .
(۲) قوله: (ومن آداب الدعاء ومن شروطه : أكل الحلال، وأن يدعو وهو موقن بالإجابة ، وأن لا يكون قلبه غافلا ، وأن لا يدعو بمحرم ، وأن لا يدعو بما هو مستحيل عادة، وأن لا يدعو إلا بما يليق الشنواني .
Section on prophecies. The specific mention of the Quran [as proof] is due to its tawatur (uninterrupted transmission) (1), not because its evidence is limited. For the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, supplicated to his Lord on many occasions, such as the day of Badr, against the killer of the people of Bi'r Ma'una, and against the mockers. The predecessors and successors have agreed upon this. Among the etiquettes of supplication (2) are: seeking virtuous times, such as during prostration and at the time of the call to prayer; and from them are performing ablution and prayer beforehand, facing the qibla, raising hands, preceding with repentance, acknowledging sin and sincerity, opening with praise and exaltation and blessings upon the Prophet, and asking by the beautiful names, and ending with blessings and peace upon him, and also placing [blessings] in the middle of it. And Allah knows best. (1) [This is] an answer to an implied question: why was the proof of supplication specified from the Quran rather than from other [texts] in the composition? And in it is a refutation of the Mu'tazila. (2) His saying: (Among the etiquettes of supplication and its conditions: eating lawful food, and that he supplicates while certain of the answer, and that his heart not be heedless, and that he not supplicate for something forbidden, and that he not supplicate for something impossible by custom, and that he not supplicate except for what is befitting... [end unclear]
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is part of a commentary on a line from Jawharat al-Tawhid. It addresses two distinct topics: why the Quran is specifically cited as proof for prophethood, and the proper etiquettes of supplication. The author first explains that the Quran's unique transmission (tawatur) is the reason it is singled out, not any limitation in its evidence. He then supports this by noting that the Prophet himself used supplication in various situations, and that there is consensus on this point. The following section lists a set of recommended practices for supplication, including choosing virtuous times, performing ablution and prayer, facing the qibla, raising hands, and beginning and ending with praise and blessings on the Prophet. The footnotes provide additional context: the first clarifies that the mention of the Quran as a proof for supplication is meant to refute the Mu'tazilite position; the second expands on the conditions of supplication, such as lawful sustenance, certainty of being heard, attentiveness, avoidance of unlawful requests, and avoiding requests that are customarily impossible. The commentary follows the author's linear exposition, moving from the proof to the practical guidelines.

Key Terms
تواتر
إجماع
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Quran is mentioned as proof only because its evidence is limited.
Correction: The author clarifies it is due to its tawatur, not limited evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the Quran specifically mentioned as proof for prophethood?
A) Because it is the only miracle
B) Due to its tawatur (uninterrupted transmission)
C) Because its evidence is limited
D) Because it refutes the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
The Quran's tawatur is the reason it is singled out as proof for prophethood, not any limitation in its evidential power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the certainty that the Quran is transmitted to you without break affect the way you respond to its commands today? If you truly believed it is from Allah, what would you do differently this week?
?
The Prophet turned to his Lord in specific moments of crisis. When you face a personal crisis, do you instinctively turn to Allah in supplication, or do you rely on other means first? What does your first reaction reveal about your belief in prophethood?
?
Scholarly consensus is cited as support for this teaching. Are there any teachings in the Quran that you have not fully accepted because they conflict with your desires? What would it take for you to submit to that teaching as the early and later scholars did?

Etiquettes of Supplication
Lists etiquettes of supplication for the believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the key etiquettes of supplication as outlined in this passage, including the recommended times, actions, and structure of the du'a.

Core Idea
Supplication has etiquettes that enhance its acceptance, including times, purification, and framing.

Having established the proof of prophethood, the author now turns to the practical aspect of supplication. He lists a set of etiquettes that the believer should observe when making du'a. These cover when to pray, how to prepare, and the content of the prayer itself. This lesson will help you memorize and apply these etiquettes.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of etiquettes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author enumerates several etiquettes beginning with 'تحري الأوقات الفاضلة'.
Footnote (2) adds more conditions, but we focus on the main list.
2
Categorize the etiquettes
Group them into three categories: times (e.g., during prostration), preparatory actions (e.g., wudu, facing qibla), and verbal framing (e.g., opening with praise).
This will help you remember them.
3
Note the inclusion of blessings on the Prophet
The author emphasizes that the du'a should be opened and closed with blessings on the Prophet, and also placed in the middle.
This is a distinctive feature of proper supplication.
4
Understand the importance of sincerity
Though not explicit in the main list, the mention of 'الإخلاص' (sincerity) is key. Reflect on why sincerity is essential.
Sincerity is a condition accepted by all scholars.

Precedence of the Prophet and Varying Levels in Gathering
Sanusi enumerates the Prophet's precedence and the varying states of people in the gathering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Prophet Muhammad is given precedence in resurrection and Paradise, and that people's ranks in the gathering vary according to their deeds.

Core Idea
The Prophet is first in resurrection and Paradise; people's gathering ranks reflect their deeds.

This lesson explores two key aspects of the resurrection as taught by Imam al-Sanusi. First, the unique precedence of Prophet Muhammad—he will be the first to be resurrected, the first to arrive at the gathering place, and the first to enter Paradise. Second, the principle that people's conditions during the gathering differ based on their deeds, with some riding, some walking, and some crawling on their faces. These points affirm both the Prophet's exalted status and the justice of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two main points: the Prophet's precedence and the variation in people's states.
2
Identify the Prophet's precedence
Notice the three 'firsts' mentioned: first to be resurrected, first to arrive at the gathering, first to enter Paradise.
This shows his unique rank among creation.
3
Understand the variation in gathering
Look at the three categories of people: rider, walking on feet, walking on face. Reflect on how they relate to deeds.
The passage states the variation is due to difference in deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَوَّلُ مَن تَنشَقُ عنه الأرض نَبيُّنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فهو أَوَّلُ مَن يُبعث ، وأوَّلُ واردٍ المَحشر، كما أنَّه أَوَّلُ دَاخل الجنَّة ، ومراتب النَّاس في الحَشْر مُتفاوتَةٌ لتفاوت مراتبهم في الأعمال ؛ فمنهم الرَّاكب ، والماشي على رجليه أو وجهه.
And the first for whom the earth splits open is our Prophet Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, so he is the first to be resurrected, and the first to arrive at the gathering place, just as he is the first to enter Paradise. And the ranks of people in the gathering are different due to the difference in their ranks in deeds; among them are the rider, and the one walking on his feet or on his face.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage establishes the precedence of Prophet Muhammad in the events of the resurrection. The author states that the Prophet is the first for whom the earth will split open, meaning he is the first to be resurrected from his grave. He is also the first to arrive at the place of gathering (mahshar) and the first to enter Paradise. This precedence is not merely chronological but reflects his highest rank among creation. Then the author notes that people's states during the gathering will vary according to their deeds. Some will be riding (indicating honor and ease), some will be walking on their feet, and some will be walking on their faces (indicating humiliation). The variation in modes of travel corresponds to the variation in their good deeds or sins. This emphasizes the justice of God: each is rewarded or punished according to what they did.

Key Terms
أول من تنشق عنه الأرض
أول من يبعث
وارد المحشر
أول داخل الجنة
مراتب الحشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Everyone will be resurrected at the same time and in the same manner.
Correction: The Prophet is first in resurrection and Paradise, and people's states vary based on their deeds.

D) The Prophet alone has a special rank

Key Takeaway
Prophet Muhammad is the first in resurrection and Paradise, and people's gathering states are justly varied according to their deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
